Allah's Existence
beginner
10 min
Creation as Evidence for God's Attributes
Razi presents a series of five theological arguments from creation's properties to God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observable properties of contingent beings are used in kalam to infer God's necessary existence, power, knowledge, will, and oneness.

Core Idea
Creation's need, precision, and uniqueness each point directly to a distinct attribute of God.

This passage condenses the classic kalam argument from contingency and design into a single sweeping sentence. Each clause draws a direct link between a feature of created things and a divine attribute. As you read, notice how the argument moves from existence to oneness, building a cumulative case for tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the repetitive structure: each clause starts with a conjunction and builds on the previous.
The Arabic is one long sentence; pay attention to the parallel structure.
2
Identify the five indications
List the five observable features mentioned: need, bestowal, precision, specification, and gathering. Each is paired with a divine attribute.
3
Trace the logical connection
For each pair, explain in your own words why that feature logically points to that attribute. For example: 'need' implies dependence on a self-sufficient being.
4
Appreciate the cumulative argument
Consider how the final clause unites all attributes while affirming God's oneness. Why is it important that the being is one?
The argument avoids multiplicity of gods by showing that all these effects converge in one being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله الذي يدل افتقار كل موجود في الوجود إليه على وجوب وجوده، وإفاضته إياه متصفا بما أمكن من الكمال على كمال قدرته وجوده، وإتقان ذلك الموجود في ذاته ونظمه مع ما سواه على علمه وحكمته، وتخصيصه بخواصه التي لا يشاركه فيها غيره على عنايته وإرادته، واجتماع هذه الآثار فيه مع كونه واحدا على وحدانيته وبراءته عن الخلل والنقصان على نفي الكثرة عن ذاته وصفاته.
Praise be to God, whose every existent's need in existence for Him indicates His necessary existence; His pouring forth upon it, qualified with as much perfection as possible, indicates the perfection of His power and generosity; the precision of that existent in its essence and its order with what is other than it indicates His knowledge and wisdom; its specification with its particular properties in which none other shares indicates His care and will; and the gathering of these effects in it while it is one indicates His oneness and its freedom from defect and deficiency indicates the negation of multiplicity from His essence and attributes.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a compact theological argument that uses the observable properties of contingent beings to infer various attributes of God. It is structured as a series of indications (dalalat). Each clause presents a different aspect of created things and links it to a specific divine attribute: (1) The need (iftiqar) of every existent for God indicates His necessary existence (wujub wujud). (2) The bestowal of existence (ifada) upon that existent, qualified with as much perfection as possible, indicates the perfection of God's power (qudra) and generosity (jud). (3) The precision (itqan) of that existent in itself and its orderly relation to others indicates God's knowledge ('ilm) and wisdom (hikma). (4) Its specification (takhsis) with unique properties (khawass) indicates God's care ('inaya) and will (irada). (5) The gathering of all these effects in one thing while it remains one indicates God's oneness (wahdaniyya) and its freedom from defect indicates the negation of multiplicity (nafy al-kathra) from His essence and attributes. This is a standard kalam argument from contingency and design to establish tawhid and the attributes.

Key Terms
افتقار
وجوب وجود
قدرة
جود
إتقان
علم
حكمة
تخصيص
عناية
إرادة
وحدانية
نفي الكثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes what it proves.
Correction: It is not circular; it uses observed features of contingent beings to infer the necessary attributes of the Creator, which is a standard inductive argument.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the precision (itqan) of an existent in its essence and order indicate?
A) God's power and generosity
B) God's knowledge and wisdom
C) God's care and will
D) God's oneness

Key Takeaway
Creation's very structure provides a multi-faceted proof for God's existence, attributes, and oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own existence, do you sense your utter need for God, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? This lesson says your need itself proves God's necessity. How does that awareness change how you start your day?
?
The lesson teaches that the precision of creation points to God's knowledge. Reflect on an intricate detail in your own life or the world that you previously dismissed as random. Will you now train your eyes to see God's wisdom in it?
?
Razi says the gathering of all effects in one thing indicates God's oneness. Do you live with a sense of God's unity in all aspects of your life, or do you compartmentalize? What would change if you truly believed that every effect comes from the One?

Allah's Existence

Proof of God's Existence and Attributes
Theological proof of divine attributes via creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author deduces God's existence, unity, and attributes from the contingency and perfections of creation.

Core Idea
All creation points to a Necessary Being with perfect attributes.

The lesson opens with a profound praise of God that is not just devotional but argumentative. Imam al-Razi presents a chain of reasoning: the neediness of every existent proves God's necessary existence, the perfections in creation prove His attributes, and the unity of traces proves His oneness. This passage is a theological foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the praise passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how each phrase connects to a divine attribute.
2
Identify the attributes deduced
List the attributes deduced: existence, power, knowledge, wisdom, will, unity, and negation of multiplicity.
3
Understand the logic
Each attribute is proven by a specific aspect of creation: neediness, perfection, order, specification, combination of traces, freedom from defect.
4
Reflect on the salawat
The blessing on the Prophet and family shows reverence and completes the introduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله الذي يدل افتقار كل موجود في الوجود إليه على وجوب وجوده، وإفاضته إياه متصفا بما أمكن من الكمال على كمال قدرته وجوده، وإتقان ذلك الموجود في ذاته ونظمه مع ما سواه على علمه وحكمته، وتخصيصه بخواصه التي لا يشاركه فيها غيره على عنايته وإرادته، واجتماع هذه الآثار فيه مع كونه واحدا على وحدانيته، وبراءته عن الخلل والنقصان على نفي الكثرة عن ذاته وصفاته. والصلاة على نبيه المبعوث للهداية، المنقذ لمتابعيه من الغواية، وعلى آله الهادين، وعترته المهديين وأصحابه المهتدين. سلام الله عليهم أجمعين. وبعد: فإن أساس العلوم الدينية علم أصول الدين، الذي يحوم سائله حول اليقين، ولا يتم بدون الخوض في سائرها كأصول الفقه وفروعه. فإن الشروع في جميعها يحتاج إلى تقديم شروعه، حتى لا يكون الخائض فيها وإن كان مقلدا لأصولها كبان على غير أساس، وإذا مثل عما هو عليه لم يقدر على إيراد حجة أو قياس. وفي هذا الزمان قد انصرفت الهمم عن تحصيل الحق بالتحقيق، وزلت الأقدام عن سواء الطريق، بحيث لا يوجد راغب في العلوم ولا خاطب للإنهياط [؟]، وصارت الطباع كأنها مجبولة على الجهل والرذيلة، إلا بقية يرمون فيما يرومون رمية رام في ليلة ظلماء، ويخبطون فيما ينحون نحوه خبط عشواء. ولم تبق في الكتب التي يتداولونها من عام الأصول حياة [؟] ولا خبر، ولا من تأكيد القواعد الحقيقية دين ولا أثر. وكتاب المحصل الذي اسمه غير مطابق لمعناه، وبيانه غير موصل إلى دعواه. وهم يزعمون أنه في ذلك العلم كاف، وعن أمراض الجهل والتقليد شاف، والحق أن فيه من الغث والرديء ما لا يحصى. والمعتمد عليه في إصابة اليقين.
Praise be to God, whose demonstration from the neediness of every existent in existence to Him indicates the necessity of His existence, and His bestowal of existence upon it qualified with what is possible of perfection indicates the perfection of His power and generosity, and the perfection of that existent in its essence and its order with what is other than it indicates His knowledge and wisdom, and its specification with its properties in which none other shares indicates His care and will, and the combination of these traces in it while it is one indicates His unity, and its being free from defect and deficiency indicates the negation of multiplicity from His essence and attributes. And blessing upon His prophet sent for guidance, the rescuer of his followers from error, and upon his guiding family, his rightly-guided household, and his guided companions. The peace of God be upon them all. And after: indeed, the foundation of the religious sciences is the science of the principles of religion (usul al-din), whose seeker hovers around certainty, and without it, delving into other sciences like usul al-fiqh and its branches is not complete. For beginning in all of them requires the presentation of its starting points, so that the one who enters into them, even if he is a follower of its principles, does not build on an unstable foundation, and if he is asked about what he holds, he is not able to present an argument or analogy. And in this time, aspirations have turned away from acquiring the truth by verification, and feet have slipped from the straight path, so that no seeker of the sciences is found nor a caller for excellence [؟], and natures have become as if created upon ignorance and vice, except a remnant who shoot in what they aim at the shot of an archer in a dark night, and who stumble in what they intend the stumbling of a blind person. And there remains in the books they circulate of the general principles no life [؟] nor experience, nor of the affirmation of the true rules any religion nor trace. And the book al-Muhassal, whose name does not match its meaning, and whose explanation does not lead to its claim. They claim that it is sufficient in that knowledge, and healing for the diseases of ignorance and blind imitation, but the truth is that it contains of the bad and worthless what cannot be counted. And the reliable source for hitting certainty is…
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage begins with a second praise of God, deducing His attributes from the contingency and neediness of all existents. The author argues that the need of every existent for a necessary being proves God's existence, and the perfections in creation prove His power, knowledge, wisdom, will, and unity. Then he states that usul al-din is the foundation of all religious sciences; without it, other fields like usul al-fiqh are baseless. He criticizes the current state of scholarship, where people have turned away from verification and follow blind imitation. He specifically attacks the book al-Muhassal (likely referring to a previous work, perhaps by al-Razi himself or another), saying its name does not match its content and it is filled with worthless material. Despite claims that it is sufficient for certainty, the author implies it is not reliable.

Key Terms
وجوب الوجود
كمال القدرة
العلم والحكمة
العناية والإرادة
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the neediness of every existent indicate?
A) God's power
B) God's knowledge
C) God's necessary existence
D) God's will

Key Takeaway
The neediness and perfections of all created beings collectively prove God's existence, attributes, and oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the unique qualities and circumstances of your own life—the specific family, talents, and trials you have—do you truly see them as signs of Allah's personal care and will for you, or do you take them for granted? What would change if you lived each day consciously aware that your very existence is a sign pointing to His attributes?
?
This passage argues that the order and perfection in creation prove Allah's knowledge and wisdom. Think of one area of your life that feels disordered or imperfect right now. How does this truth about Allah's wisdom challenge you to trust Him in that area, even when you cannot see the wisdom?
?
The absence of defect in creation points to Allah being free from multiplicity—He is simple, not composed of parts. Does your worship reflect this belief, or do you sometimes treat Allah as if He has competing attributes or parts? How can you cultivate a more unified devotion that recognizes His absolute oneness in both essence and attributes?

The Foundation of Usul al-Din and Critique of Scholarship
Declaration of usul al-din's foundational role and critique of scholarship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why usul al-din is the foundation of religious sciences and how the author criticizes the state of scholarship and the book al-Muhassal.

Core Idea
Without usul al-din, all religious knowledge is baseless.

After the theological preamble, Imam al-Razi turns to the state of religious knowledge. He declares that usul al-din (principles of religion) is the foundation upon which all other religious sciences rest. Without it, fields like usul al-fiqh are incomplete. He then laments that scholars of his time have abandoned verification for blind imitation, and he specifically criticizes the book al-Muhassal as insufficient and filled with worthless content.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on usul al-din as foundation
Understand that usul al-din is essential for certainty and for defending faith.
2
Identify the critique of current scholarship
Notice the strong language: aspirations turned away, feet slipped, natures created upon ignorance.
3
Examine the criticism of al-Muhassal
The author says its name doesn't match meaning, its explanation doesn't lead to its claim, and it contains much bad material.
4
Reflect on the consequence
The reliable source for hitting certainty is not al-Muhassal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله الذي يدل افتقار كل موجود في الوجود إليه على وجوب وجوده، وإفاضته إياه متصفا بما أمكن من الكمال على كمال قدرته وجوده، وإتقان ذلك الموجود في ذاته ونظمه مع ما سواه على علمه وحكمته، وتخصيصه بخواصه التي لا يشاركه فيها غيره على عنايته وإرادته، واجتماع هذه الآثار فيه مع كونه واحدا على وحدانيته، وبراءته عن الخلل والنقصان على نفي الكثرة عن ذاته وصفاته. والصلاة على نبيه المبعوث للهداية، المنقذ لمتابعيه من الغواية، وعلى آله الهادين، وعترته المهديين وأصحابه المهتدين. سلام الله عليهم أجمعين. وبعد: فإن أساس العلوم الدينية علم أصول الدين، الذي يحوم سائله حول اليقين، ولا يتم بدون الخوض في سائرها كأصول الفقه وفروعه. فإن الشروع في جميعها يحتاج إلى تقديم شروعه، حتى لا يكون الخائض فيها وإن كان مقلدا لأصولها كبان على غير أساس، وإذا مثل عما هو عليه لم يقدر على إيراد حجة أو قياس. وفي هذا الزمان قد انصرفت الهمم عن تحصيل الحق بالتحقيق، وزلت الأقدام عن سواء الطريق، بحيث لا يوجد راغب في العلوم ولا خاطب للإنهياط [؟]، وصارت الطباع كأنها مجبولة على الجهل والرذيلة، إلا بقية يرمون فيما يرومون رمية رام في ليلة ظلماء، ويخبطون فيما ينحون نحوه خبط عشواء. ولم تبق في الكتب التي يتداولونها من عام الأصول حياة [؟] ولا خبر، ولا من تأكيد القواعد الحقيقية دين ولا أثر. وكتاب المحصل الذي اسمه غير مطابق لمعناه، وبيانه غير موصل إلى دعواه. وهم يزعمون أنه في ذلك العلم كاف، وعن أمراض الجهل والتقليد شاف، والحق أن فيه من الغث والرديء ما لا يحصى. والمعتمد عليه في إصابة اليقين.
Praise be to God, whose demonstration from the neediness of every existent in existence to Him indicates the necessity of His existence, and His bestowal of existence upon it qualified with what is possible of perfection indicates the perfection of His power and generosity, and the perfection of that existent in its essence and its order with what is other than it indicates His knowledge and wisdom, and its specification with its properties in which none other shares indicates His care and will, and the combination of these traces in it while it is one indicates His unity, and its being free from defect and deficiency indicates the negation of multiplicity from His essence and attributes. And blessing upon His prophet sent for guidance, the rescuer of his followers from error, and upon his guiding family, his rightly-guided household, and his guided companions. The peace of God be upon them all. And after: indeed, the foundation of the religious sciences is the science of the principles of religion (usul al-din), whose seeker hovers around certainty, and without it, delving into other sciences like usul al-fiqh and its branches is not complete. For beginning in all of them requires the presentation of its starting points, so that the one who enters into them, even if he is a follower of its principles, does not build on an unstable foundation, and if he is asked about what he holds, he is not able to present an argument or analogy. And in this time, aspirations have turned away from acquiring the truth by verification, and feet have slipped from the straight path, so that no seeker of the sciences is found nor a caller for excellence [؟], and natures have become as if created upon ignorance and vice, except a remnant who shoot in what they aim at the shot of an archer in a dark night, and who stumble in what they intend the stumbling of a blind person. And there remains in the books they circulate of the general principles no life [؟] nor experience, nor of the affirmation of the true rules any religion nor trace. And the book al-Muhassal, whose name does not match its meaning, and whose explanation does not lead to its claim. They claim that it is sufficient in that knowledge, and healing for the diseases of ignorance and blind imitation, but the truth is that it contains of the bad and worthless what cannot be counted. And the reliable source for hitting certainty is…

Commentary
This passage begins with a second praise of God, deducing His attributes from the contingency and neediness of all existents. The author argues that the need of every existent for a necessary being proves God's existence, and the perfections in creation prove His power, knowledge, wisdom, will, and unity. Despite claims that it is sufficient for certainty, the author implies it is not reliable.

Key Terms
علم أصول الدين
اليقين
كتاب المحصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Muhassal is a reliable and sufficient book for usul al-din.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the book contains much worthless material and does not lead to its claim; it is not the reliable source for certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the author say about the book al-Muhassal?
A) It is the best book on usul al-din.
B) Its name matches its meaning and it leads to certainty.
C) It contains much worthless material and does not lead to its claim.
D) It is sufficient for all students.

Key Takeaway
The author declares usul al-din as the essential foundation of religious knowledge and strongly criticizes the reliance on the book al-Muhassal and the general decline in verification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you currently building your religious knowledge on a foundation of verification (tahqiq) or on blind imitation (taqlid)? Take a moment to honestly assess: if someone asked you tomorrow to prove that Allah exists, could you present a clear argument, or would you stumble like the 'remnant shooting in the dark'?
?
The author criticizes scholars who rely on books like al-Muhassal instead of verifying the truth for themselves. What books or teachers do you trust for your understanding of faith? Are you confident they are reliable, or are you following a tradition without questioning its foundation?
?
This passage warns that without usul al-din, even a follower cannot defend his position. When was the last time you felt spiritually unprepared to answer a doubt about your creed? Will you commit to learning the foundational proofs so that you are no longer caught off guard?

Allah's Existence
advanced
8 min
Two Meanings of Existence and the Essence-Existence Puzzle
Razi discusses the two meanings of existence/non-existence and refutes the identity of essence and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two senses of 'affirmation' and 'negation' in kalam and the metaphysical puzzles regarding the relationship between essence and existence, as articulated by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Essence and existence: identical, different, or both? Razi shows the dead ends.

This passage from Imam Razi's Muhassal presents a third argument that delves into the two senses of existence and non-existence. Before we can speak of a thing's existence, we must clarify what we mean by 'existence' and how it relates to the thing's essence. Razi systematically examines the possibilities and reveals the logical pitfalls that arise from each view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the Arabic may have OCR errors; the commentary clarifies.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of affirmation/negation
Identify the two senses: 'thing in itself' (e.g., blackness exists or not) and 'thing for another' (e.g., body is black or not). Notice how the first sense leads to the core puzzle.
This sets the stage for the analysis of 'blackness exists'.
3
Analyze the case of 'blackness exists'
Razi breaks down 'blackness exists' into two possibilities: being blackness is identical to being existence, or different. Examine why both lead to problems.
The identity option is dismissed as redundant; the difference option leads to two objections.
4
Examine the two objections against difference
Read the two objections carefully: (1) existence as a property in a non-existent substrate, causing absurdities; (2) the problem of referring to two different referents when predicating existence.
These objections are central to the argument.
5
Understand Razi's marginal comments and dismissal
In the latter part, Razi responds to anticipated replies: he argues the division is not exhaustive, existence itself is not an existent property, and the opponent's position is incoherent.
These remarks refute the opponent's attempts to avoid the objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لنها : لو سلمنا الامتياز لكان الإثبات والنفى قد يكون المراد منهما ثبوت الشيء في نفسه أو عدمه في نفسه كقولنا السواد إما أن يكون موجوداً وإما أن لا يكون موجودا ، وقد يكون المراد منهما ثبوت الشوء لشيء آخر وعدمه عنه. كقولنا : الجسم وإما أن يكون أسود وإما أن لا يكون . أما الأول : من المعلوم بالضرورة إن قولنا السواد إما أن يكون موجودا وإما أن لا يكون موجودا لا يمكن التصديق به إلا بعد تصور مفهوم قولنا السواد موجود السواد معدوم ، ولكن كل واحد منهما باطل . أما الأول : فلانا إذا قلنا السواد موجود فإما أن يكون كونه موادا هو نفس كونه موجودا أو مغايرا له . فإن كان الأول كان قولنا السواد موجود جاريا مجرى قولنا السواد سواد وقولنا الموجود موجود ومعلوم أنه ليس كذلك لأن هذا الأخير هذر ، والأول مفيد . وإن كان الثاني فهو باطل من وجهين . أحدهما: إذا كان الوجود قائما بالسواد فالسواد في نفسه ليس بموجود وإلا لعاد البحث فيه ، ولمكان الشيء الواحد موجودا مرتين وإذا كان كذلك كان الوجود قائما بما ليس بموجود ، لكن الوجود صفة وإلا لنبت الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم وأنتم أنكرتموه ، فحينئد يكون الصفة الموجودة محالة في محل معدوم وذلك غير معقول ، إذ لو جاز ذلك الجاز أن يكون محل هذه الأنوان والحركات غير موجودة وذلك موجب الشك في وجود الأجسام وهو عين السفسطه . الثاني : أنه إذا كان الوجود مغايراً للماهية كان مسمى قولنا السواد غير مسمى قولنا موجود فإذا قلنا السواد موجود بمعنى أن السواد هو موجود كان ذلك حكما بوحدة الاثنين وهو محال ، فإن قلت ليس المراد من قولنا السواد موجود وهو أن مسمى السواد مسمى الوجود ، بل المراد أن السواد موصوف بالموجودية قلت فحينئذ ينقل الكلام إلى مسمى الموصوفية ، فإنه إما أن يكون مسمى السواد هو مسمى الموصوفية بالوجود محال فيكون قولنا السواد موصوف بالوجود جاريا مجرى قولنا السواد سواد وهو محال ، وإما أن يكون مغايرا له . أقول : الكائن سوادا هو غير الكائن موجودا والسواد مغاير للوجود وذلك لأن ههنا شيئاً واحداً يقال له تارة إنه سواد وتارة إنه موجود ، فالمقول عليه منهما واحد ، والمقولان متغايران ، فإذا القسمة أن كون أحدهما عين الآخر أو مغايرا له ليست بحاصرة ويعوزه قسم آخر وهو أن يكونا متحدتين من وجه و متغايرتين من جهة أخرى . أقول : لا يلزم من كون المتغايرة قيام أحدهما بالآخر فإنها إذا قبل الحيوان جسم لا يلزم منه قيام الجسم بالحيوان ، وأيضا لا يلزم من كون الوجود فائما بالسواد كون السواد في نفسه معدوما، وإذا كان السواد في نفسه لا موجودا ولا معدوما لم يعد البحث ولم يكن الشيء الواحد موجودا مرتين ، وليس الوجود صفة موجودة ، فإن ذلك يقتضى ثبوت وجود الوجوه ويتسلسل ولا يلزم من سلب صفة الوجود عن الوجود ثبوت العدم له أو ثبوت الواسطة ، فإن ذلك إنما يلزم لاحظة نفى الوجود أو العدم أو سلبهما مع مفهوم الوجود ، وحين نلاحظ نفس الوجود لا مع ملاحظه الغير لم يلزم ذلك ولا يلزم من ملاحظه الغير لم يازم ذلك ولا يلزم من ذلك كون الألوان والحركات بمحل غير موجود ، فإن كون الوجود حالا في محل غير موجود يقتضى كون اللون والحركة حالين في محل غير ملون ولا نحرك ، وظاهر أن جميع ما قاله فى هذا الموضع خبط لا يليق إيراده بأمثاله .
And the third: even if we grant the distinction, affirmation and negation may be intended to mean the thing's existence in itself or its non-existence in itself – like our statement 'blackness either exists or does not exist' – or they may be intended to mean the thing's existence for another or its non-existence from another – like our statement 'the body either is black or is not'. As for the first: it is known necessarily that our statement 'blackness either exists or does not exist' cannot be judged except after conceiving the concept of our statement 'blackness exists' and 'blackness does not exist', but each of them is false. As for the first: when we say 'blackness exists', either its being blackness is identical to its being existence or different. If the first, then our statement 'blackness exists' is like our statement 'blackness is blackness' and 'the existent is existent', and it is known that that is not the case because the latter is redundant (hadhr) and the former is informative (mufid). If the second, it is false in two ways: One: if existence subsists in blackness, then blackness in itself is not existent, otherwise the discussion would return, and the same thing would exist twice; and if so, existence would subsist in what is not existent, but existence is a property (sifa), otherwise the intermediary between existent and non-existent would be affirmed, and you have denied it; then the existent property would inhere in a non-existent locus, which is unintelligible, for if that were permissible, then the locus of these colors and motions could be non-existent, which would cause doubt about the existence of bodies, and that is pure sophistry (safsata). Two: if existence is different from essence (mahiyya), then the referent of our statement 'blackness' is not the referent of our statement 'existent'; so if we say 'blackness exists' meaning that blackness is existence, that is a judgment of the unity of two things, which is impossible. If you say: the meaning of our statement 'blackness exists' is not that the referent of blackness is the referent of existence, but rather that blackness is described by existence, then the discussion shifts to the referent of 'being described' (mawsufiyya); either the referent of blackness is the referent of being described by existence – impossible, so our statement 'blackness is described by existence' would be like 'blackness is blackness' which is impossible – or it is different. I say: that which is blackness is not that which is existence, and blackness is different from existence, because here there is one thing that is sometimes called blackness and sometimes called existent; the subject (maqul 'alayhi) of both is one, but the predicates (maqulan) are different. So the division that one is identical to the other or different from it is not exhaustive; it needs another division: that they are united in one respect and different in another. I say: it does not follow from their difference that one subsists in the other; for when animal is receptive of body, it does not follow that body subsists in animal. Also, it does not follow from existence subsisting in blackness that blackness is in itself non-existent; if blackness is in itself neither existent nor non-existent, the discussion does not return, nor is the one thing existent twice. And existence is not an existent property, for that would require the establishment of existence of existence and an infinite regress. And it does not follow from negating the property of existence from existence the affirmation of non-existence for it or the affirmation of an intermediary; for that only follows if one considers the negation of existence or non-existence or both together with the concept of existence; when we consider existence itself without considering something else, it does not follow. And it does not follow from that that colors and motions are in a non-existent locus; for existence being in a non-existent locus would require that color and motion be in a locus that is not colored or moving, and it is clear that all that he said in this place is incoherent and not worthy of being presented by his likes.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (thālithahā) regarding the two meanings of affirmation (ithbāt) and negation (nafy). The first meaning is existence or non-existence of the thing in itself (e.g., 'blackness exists or does not exist'), and the second is the thing's possession or lack of a property for another (e.g., 'the body is black or not'). Focusing on the first meaning, Razi argues that the judgment 'blackness exists or does not exist' requires conceiving both 'blackness exists' and 'blackness does not exist'. However, each of these is false. Analyzing 'blackness exists', either its blackness is identical to its existence or different. If identical, the statement is redundant like 'blackness is blackness' and uninformative. If different, two problems arise: (1) If existence subsists in blackness, then blackness in itself is not existent, leading to a regress and the absurdity of an existent property in a non-existent substrate, which undermines the reality of bodies and leads to sophistry. (2) If existence is different from essence, then the referents differ, so to say 'blackness exists' either affirms identity (impossible) or shifts to the attribute of 'being described', which also leads to redundancy or difference. Razi adds marginal comments: he refutes the exhaustive nature of the division (identity vs. difference) by proposing that subject and predicate can be united in one respect and different in another. He also denies that if existence subsists in blackness, blackness must be non-existent; it can be neutral (neither existent nor non-existent). He further argues that existence itself is not an existent property to avoid infinite regress, and that negating existence from existence does not imply non-existence or an intermediary; it only appears so if one conflates the concept with its negation. Finally, he dismisses the opponent's entire objection as incoherent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is a simple property that can be added to an essence without metaphysical complications.
Correction: Razi shows that treating existence as either identical to or different from essence leads to logical absurdities, indicating the relationship is more complex.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's analysis, what is one consequence of existence being different from essence (mahiyya)?
A) The statement 'blackness exists' becomes redundant.
B) Existence, as a property, would inhere in a non-existent locus.
C) Blackness would become a universal concept.
D) The intellect cannot conceive blackness.

Key Takeaway
The relationship between essence and existence is not straightforward; both identity and difference lead to logical difficulties, and Razi ultimately finds the opponent's position incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that even a simple statement like 'blackness exists' is fraught with philosophical pitfalls. How often do you assume that your understanding of basic realities is straightforward, and what does this teach you about the need for humility in seeking knowledge of Allah's attributes? Will you commit to approaching theological questions with this same humility?
?
The discussion warns against sophistry and the doubt it can cause about the existence of bodies. In your own life, have you ever let philosophical doubts or overanalysis creep into your certainty about the fundamentals of faith? What specific safeguard will you put in place to protect your faith from such doubts?
?
Razi dismisses an opponent's argument as 'incoherent and not worthy of being presented by his likes.' When you encounter a weak argument against your beliefs, do you respond with dismissiveness or with patient refutation? Objection and Response on Theological Reasoning
Author presents one objection and responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand one classical objection to using theoretical reason in theology and the way it is addressed in this text, including the apparent tension in the response.

Core Idea
The objection claims divine realities are inconceivable, but the response seems to accept that conclusion.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a critique of theological reasoning. The objection argues that since we cannot conceive divine realities, we cannot seek knowledge about them. The author's response appears to concede this, which raises questions about his full argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the objection is framed and how the author responds.
This passage is part of a larger discussion; the author's full refutation may continue elsewhere.
2
Identify the objection's logic
The objection claims that seeking affirmation (taṣdīq) depends on conception (taṣawwur) of subject and predicate, and divine realities are inconceivable. Trace the reasoning: if no conception, then no affirmation.
3
Examine the author's response
The author responds by referencing that we only conceive what we sense, introspect, or understand intellectually. He then concludes that if conception is lost, affirmation is also impossible.
This response appears to accept the objection's premise.
4
Notice the puzzling conclusion
The author's conclusion seems to agree with the objector—that affirmation is impossible. This suggests either a concession or a rhetorical step in a larger argument.
The full argument likely includes a rebuttal that we do have some conception of divine attributes through effects.
5
Reflect on the larger context

The lesson title suggests a refutation, so this is likely only part of the picture.

Commentary
This passage presents one of the two objections to the possibility of theoretical reasoning in theology, along with the author's response. The objection states that to seek knowledge of a proposition, one must already have a conception of its subject and predicate; but divine realities are not conceivable. The author responds by referencing an earlier point: we only conceive what we can sense, introspect, or intellectually grasp. If conception is impossible, then affirmation (taṣdīq) is also impossible. This appears to be a partial refutation, accepting the premise but concluding that if conception fails, then certainly affirmation fails — which might actually support the objector. However, the full refutation likely continues elsewhere.

The Independence of Reason in Knowing God
Razi states the issue and gives his contingency proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's straightforward argument that knowledge of God's existence does not require a teacher, based on the contingency of the world.

Core Idea
Reason alone can prove God's existence—no teacher needed.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi tackles a bold claim: that even without a teacher, a person can come to know God. He provides a simple logical proof from the contingency of the world, asserting that once we understand that the world is contingent and that every contingent requires an effecter, we know God—whether or not a teacher is present. Let's examine this core argument before considering the objections he addresses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the direct, assertive tone of the issue statement.
The passage begins with 'Issue:' which signals a formal theological problem.
2
Identify the proof
Look for the conditional sentence: 'when knowledge occurs that the world is contingent and every contingent has an effecter, we know that the world has an effecter.' This is the entire proof—simple and direct.
Razi does not elaborate further here; the proof is self-contained.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that this knowledge occurs 'whether there is a teacher or not.' The teacher is irrelevant to the validity of the proof.
This directly contradicts the atheist view that a teacher is necessary.
4
Reflect on the link
Consider that the proof relies on two premises: (1) the world is contingent, and (2) every contingent needs an effecter. If both are known by reason alone, then the conclusion follows without external aid.
Razi does not argue why these premises are known—he assumes they are self-evident or established elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا حاجة في معرفة الله تعالى وتقدس إلى المعلم خلافا للملاحدة لعنهم الله تعالى . لنا أنه متى حصل العلم بأن العالم ممكن وكل ممكن فله مؤثر علمنا أن العالم له مؤثر سواء كان هناك معلم أم لا ، واعتمد الجمهور منا ومن المعتزلة في إبطال قولهم على أمرين. أحدهما : أن حصول العلم بالشيء او افتقر إلى المعلم لافتة علمه بكونه معلما إلى معلم آخر ولزم التسلسل . والثانى : أنا لا نعلم كون المعلم صادقا إلا بعد العلم بأن الله تعالى صدقه واسطة إظهار المعجزة على يده ، فلو توقف العلم بالله سبحانه وتعالى على قوله لزم الدور ، فهذان الوجهان ضعيفان عندى . أما الأول : فلاحتمال أن يكون عقل الذي والإمام أكمل من عقول سائر الناس ، فلا جرم كان عقله مستقلا بإدراك الحقائق وعقل غيره لم يكن مستقلا وكان محتاجا إلى التعليم . وأما الثاني : فلان ذلك إما يلزم على من يقول العمل معزول [؟] مطلنما [؟] وقول المعلم وحده منعميد [؟] للعلم ، أما يقول العقل لابد منه لكنه غير كاف بل لا بد منه من معلم آخر يرشدنا إلى الأدلة ويوقفنا على الجواب في الشبهات، ولا يلزم منه ذلك لانا نقول : عقولنا غير مستقلة بمعرفة الدلائل والجواب عن الشبهات، فلا بد من إمام يعلمنا تلك الأدلة والأجوبة حتى أنا بواسطة تعليمه وقوة عقلنا نعرف تلك الحقائق ، ومن جملة تلك الحقائق أن يعلمنا ما يدل على إمامته وعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم الدور والتسلسل . واحتجوا بأنا نرى الاختلاف مستمرا بين أهل العالم ولوكفى العقل لما كان كذلك . ونرى أن الإنسان وحده لا يستقل بتحصيل أضعف العلوم بل لا بدله من أستاذ يهديه ، وذلك يدل على أن العقل غير كافي والجواب عن الأول : أن من أتى بالنظر على الوجه المذكور لا يعرف له ما ذكرت وعن الثاني أنه لا نزاع في السر لكن الامتناع ممنوع وإلا لزم ال ثم إنا نطالبهم بتعين ذلك الإمام وتبين أنه من أجل الناس .
Issue: There is no need in knowing God, exalted and sanctified, for a teacher, contrary to the atheists—may God curse them. We have that when knowledge occurs that the world is contingent and every contingent has an effecter, we know that the world has an effecter whether there is a teacher or not. The majority of us and the Mu'tazila relied in refuting their statement on two things: First, that the occurrence of knowledge of a thing, if it needs a teacher, then his knowledge that he is a teacher would need another teacher, leading to regress. Second, that we do not know that the teacher is truthful except after knowing that God verified him by means of manifesting a miracle at his hand; so if knowledge of God, exalted, depended on his statement, circularity would follow. Both these points are weak in my view. As for the first: because of the possibility that the intellect of the one and the Imam is more perfect than the intellects of other people; thus his intellect was independent in perceiving truths, while the intellect of others was not independent and was in need of teaching. As for the second: because that either follows for one who says action is isolated [?] and the teacher's statement alone is [?] for knowledge; but for one who says reason is necessary but insufficient, rather there must be another teacher to guide us to proofs and acquaint us with answers to doubts, it does not follow—for we say: Our intellects are not independent in knowing proofs and answering doubts, so there must be an Imam who teaches us those proofs and answers, so that by his teaching and the strength of our intellect we come to know those truths. And among those truths is that he teaches us what indicates his imamate; on this estimation, circularity and regress do not follow. And they argued that we see continuous disagreement among the people of the world, and if reason sufficed it would not be so. And we see that a person alone cannot acquire even the weakest sciences, but rather he must have a teacher who guides him; that indicates that reason is not sufficient. The answer to the first: that whoever engages in contemplation in the mentioned manner, there is not for him what you mentioned. And about the second: that there is no dispute about the secret, but impossibility is denied, otherwise it would lead to [?]. Then we demand from them the specification of that Imam and the clarification that he is from the most excellent of people.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents a theological issue (mas'ala): whether knowledge of God requires a teacher. Razi takes the position that no teacher is needed, contrary to the atheists. He provides a proof: once we know the world is contingent and every contingent has an effecter (cause), we know God's existence whether or not a teacher is present. The majority of Sunni theologians and Mu'tazila argue against the atheists using two points: the first is that if knowledge depends on a teacher, then the teacher's own knowledge would require another teacher, leading to infinite regress. The second is that we cannot know a teacher is truthful without first knowing God (who confirms the teacher through miracles), so using the teacher's claim to prove God is circular. However, Razi considers both arguments weak. For the first, he says it is possible that the intellect of the prophet or Imam is more perfect than others, so he independently perceives truths while others need teaching. For the second, he says the circularity only follows if one denies the role of reason; but if one holds that reason is necessary but insufficient, and that a teacher guides reason without being the sole source, then no circularity occurs—the teacher's proofs help reason attain knowledge, including the proof of his own imamate. Razi then addresses two objections from the atheists: (1) persistent disagreement among people shows reason does not suffice, and (2) individuals cannot acquire even simple sciences without a teacher. He responds to the first by saying that one who contemplates properly does not have the problem of disagreement; to the second, he notes that while learning from others is common, it does not prove impossibility of independent knowledge, and he challenges the atheists to specify the required Imam.

Key Terms
مسألة
ممكن
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi denies any value or need for teachers in acquiring knowledge.
Correction: Razi only denies that a teacher is necessary for the basic proof of God's existence. He elsewhere acknowledges that teachers can guide and strengthen intellect, but the core rational proof is self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Razi's proof that we can know God without a teacher?
A) The fact that prophets exist.
B) The contingency of the world and the need for an effecter.
C) The fact that atheists disagree among themselves.
D) The miracle of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The contingency of the world alone, understood by reason, suffices to prove the existence of God—no teacher is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi's Critique of Regress and Circularity Arguments
Razi critiques the majority's two arguments and offers his own theory of guided reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the two standard arguments (regress and circularity) used by theologians against atheists, and his alternative view that a teacher can guide reason without logical flaw.

Core Idea
Teacher guides reason, but reason remains the ultimate knower.

After presenting his own proof, Razi examines the arguments commonly used by Sunni and Mu'tazili theologians to refute the atheist claim that a teacher is necessary. These arguments—based on regress and circularity—are considered weak by Razi. He explains why, and then offers a more nuanced position: the intellect is not fully independent, so a teacher (like an Imam) can help, but this does not lead to regress or circularity because the teacher's proofs activate reason. Let's explore this sophisticated interaction between reason and teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the majority's arguments
Read the section where Razi presents the two arguments used by the majority: the regress argument and the circularity argument.
These are standard objections: if knowledge depended on a teacher, that teacher's knowledge would require another teacher ad infinitum; and if we need a teacher to know God, we cannot verify the teacher's truthfulness without already knowing God.
2
Understand why Razi finds them weak
Razi says both are weak. For the first, he notes that a prophet or Imam may have a more perfect intellect that independently perceives truths. For the second, he distinguishes between two views: one that denies reason entirely, and another that sees reason as necessary but insufficient—the latter avoids circularity because the teacher's guidance helps reason reach knowledge, including the proof of the teacher's own authority.
Pay attention to the phrase 'عقله مستقلا بإدراك الحقائق' – his intellect independent in perceiving truths.
3
Examine Razi's own solution
Razi explains that our intellects are not independent, so we need an Imam to teach us proofs and answers. But through his teaching and our reason, we come to know truths, including the Imam's own imamate. This prevents regress and circularity because the teacher's role is to guide, not to replace, reason.
This is a middle path: reason is necessary but not sufficient; teaching supplies the missing guidance.
4
Reflect on the atheist objections and responses
Razi then addresses two atheist objections: (1) disagreement among people shows reason is insufficient, and (2) people cannot learn even simple sciences without a teacher. He answers by saying proper contemplation removes disagreement, and the need for a teacher in many fields does not prove impossibility of independent knowledge of God.
The text becomes unclear at points due to OCR issues, but the gist is clear.

Source Text

مسألة : لا حاجة في معرفة الله تعالى وتقدس إلى المعلم خلافا للملاحدة لعنهم الله تعالى . لنا أنه متى حصل العلم بأن العالم ممكن وكل ممكن فله مؤثر علمنا أن العالم له مؤثر سواء كان هناك معلم أم لا ، واعتمد الجمهور منا ومن المعتزلة في إبطال قولهم على أمرين. أحدهما : أن حصول العلم بالشيء او افتقر إلى المعلم لافتة علمه بكونه معلما إلى معلم آخر ولزم التسلسل . والثانى : أنا لا نعلم كون المعلم صادقا إلا بعد العلم بأن الله تعالى صدقه واسطة إظهار المعجزة على يده ، فلو توقف العلم بالله سبحانه وتعالى على قوله لزم الدور ، فهذان الوجهان ضعيفان عندى . أما الأول : فلاحتمال أن يكون عقل الذي والإمام أكمل من عقول سائر الناس ، فلا جرم كان عقله مستقلا بإدراك الحقائق وعقل غيره لم يكن مستقلا وكان محتاجا إلى التعليم . وأما الثاني : فلان ذلك إما يلزم على من يقول العمل معزول [؟] مطلنما [؟] وقول المعلم وحده منعميد [؟] للعلم ، أما يقول العقل لابد منه لكنه غير كاف بل لا بد منه من معلم آخر يرشدنا إلى الأدلة ويوقفنا على الجواب في الشبهات، ولا يلزم منه ذلك لانا نقول : عقولنا غير مستقلة بمعرفة الدلائل والجواب عن الشبهات، فلا بد من إمام يعلمنا تلك الأدلة والأجوبة حتى أنا بواسطة تعليمه وقوة عقلنا نعرف تلك الحقائق ، ومن جملة تلك الحقائق أن يعلمنا ما يدل على إمامته وعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم الدور والتسلسل . واحتجوا بأنا نرى الاختلاف مستمرا بين أهل العالم ولوكفى العقل لما كان كذلك . ونرى أن الإنسان وحده لا يستقل بتحصيل أضعف العلوم بل لا بدله من أستاذ يهديه ، وذلك يدل على أن العقل غير كافي والجواب عن الأول : أن من أتى بالنظر على الوجه المذكور لا يعرف له ما ذكرت وعن الثاني أنه لا نزاع في السر لكن الامتناع ممنوع وإلا لزم ال ثم إنا نطالبهم بتعين ذلك الإمام وتبين أنه من أجل الناس .
Issue: There is no need in knowing God, exalted and sanctified, for a teacher, contrary to the atheists—may God curse them. We have that when knowledge occurs that the world is contingent and every contingent has an effecter, we know that the world has an effecter whether there is a teacher or not. The majority of us and the Mu'tazila relied in refuting their statement on two things: First, that the occurrence of knowledge of a thing, if it needs a teacher, then his knowledge that he is a teacher would need another teacher, leading to regress. Second, that we do not know that the teacher is truthful except after knowing that God verified him by means of manifesting a miracle at his hand; so if knowledge of God, exalted, depended on his statement, circularity would follow. Both these points are weak in my view. As for the first: because of the possibility that the intellect of the one and the Imam is more perfect than the intellects of other people; thus his intellect was independent in perceiving truths, while the intellect of others was not independent and was in need of teaching. As for the second: because that either follows for one who says action is isolated [?] and the teacher's statement alone is [?] for knowledge; but for one who says reason is necessary but insufficient, rather there must be another teacher to guide us to proofs and acquaint us with answers to doubts, it does not follow—for we say: Our intellects are not independent in knowing proofs and answering doubts, so there must be an Imam who teaches us those proofs and answers, so that by his teaching and the strength of our intellect we come to know those truths. And among those truths is that he teaches us what indicates his imamate; on this estimation, circularity and regress do not follow. And they argued that we see continuous disagreement among the people of the world, and if reason sufficed it would not be so. And we see that a person alone cannot acquire even the weakest sciences, but rather he must have a teacher who guides him; that indicates that reason is not sufficient. The answer to the first: that whoever engages in contemplation in the mentioned manner, there is not for him what you mentioned. And about the second: that there is no dispute about the secret, but impossibility is denied, otherwise it would lead to [?]. Then we demand from them the specification of that Imam and the clarification that he is from the most excellent of people.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents a theological issue (mas'ala): whether knowledge of God requires a teacher. Razi takes the position that no teacher is needed, contrary to the atheists. He provides a proof: once we know the world is contingent and every contingent has an effecter (cause), we know God's existence whether or not a teacher is present. The majority of Sunni theologians and Mu'tazila argue against the atheists using two points: the first is that if knowledge depends on a teacher, then the teacher's own knowledge would require another teacher, leading to infinite regress. The second is that we cannot know a teacher is truthful without first knowing God (who confirms the teacher through miracles), so using the teacher's claim to prove God is circular. However, Razi considers both arguments weak. For the first, he says it is possible that the intellect of the prophet or Imam is more perfect than others, so he independently perceives truths while others need teaching. For the second, he says the circularity only follows if one denies the role of reason; but if one holds that reason is necessary but insufficient, and that a teacher guides reason without being the sole source, then no circularity occurs—the teacher's proofs help reason attain knowledge, including the proof of his own imamate. Razi then addresses two objections from the atheists: (1) persistent disagreement among people shows reason does not suffice, and (2) individuals cannot acquire even simple sciences without a teacher. He responds to the first by saying that one who contemplates properly does not have the problem of disagreement; to the second, he notes that while learning from others is common, it does not prove impossibility of independent knowledge, and he challenges the atheists to specify the required Imam.

Key Terms
مسألة
ممكن
مؤثر
معلم
دور
تسلسل
إمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the only two options are either pure reason or pure authority (teacher).
Correction: Razi presents a third option: reason is necessary but insufficient, and a teacher supplies the missing guidance without replacing reason.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the regress argument weak?
A) Because it is impossible to have an infinite chain.
B) Because a prophet or Imam may have an independent intellect.
C) Because the argument applies only to the atheists, not to Muslims.
D) Because the teacher can be known by miracle alone.

Key Takeaway
A teacher can guide reason to knowledge of God without logical problems, provided reason is acknowledged as necessary but not fully independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you honestly believe that your own intellect is fully capable of arriving at all truths on its own, or do you recognize a need for teachers and guides? What does that reveal about your humility before Allah?
?
When you encounter a doubt or contradiction in your faith, do you try to resolve it entirely by your own reasoning, or do you actively seek out a knowledgeable teacher? What might be the consequence of relying solely on your own intellect?
?
If Razi is correct that reason is necessary but insufficient, what does that mean for the way you approach learning your deen? Are you content with being a self-taught Muslim, or are you willing to submit to a qualified teacher even when it challenges your own conclusions?

Razi's Proof and the Two Majority Arguments
Razi states his proof and presents two majority arguments for refuting atheists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's direct argument that knowledge of God does not require a teacher, as well as the two arguments from the majority (Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) used to refute the atheist position.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God's existence can be attained through reason alone, without a teacher.

Razi begins this mas'ala by stating his own position: knowledge of God does not require a teacher. He then presents his proof based on contingency and the need for an effecter. Afterward, he reports the two arguments commonly used by the majority of theologians to refute the atheist claim that a teacher is necessary. These arguments will serve as the basis for his subsequent critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement and Razi's proof
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice how Razi states his view and then gives a logical proof from contingency.
2
Identify the two arguments of the majority
Find the two arguments that the majority (Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) used against the atheists. They are introduced with 'أحدهما' and 'والثانى'.
These arguments involve infinite regress and circularity.
3
Note Razi's initial judgment
Razi concludes this section by saying these two aspects are weak in his view. This sets up his critique in the next lesson.

Question: There is no need in knowing God, Exalted and Sanctified, for a teacher, contrary to the atheists, may God curse them. Our proof: when knowledge is obtained that the world is contingent and every contingent has an effecter, we know that the world has an effecter whether there is a teacher or not. And the majority of us and of the Mu'tazila relied in refuting their statement on two matters. First: that obtaining knowledge of a thing, if it depends on a teacher, then the teacher's knowledge of being a teacher depends on another teacher, leading to infinite regress. Second: we do not know that the teacher is truthful except after knowing that God, Exalted, has verified him through the manifestation of a miracle at his hand; so if knowledge of God, Glorified and Exalted, depended on his statement, circularity would follow. These two aspects are weak in my view. As for the first: because it is possible that the intellect of the one who is the imam is more complete than the intellects of other people; thus his intellect is independent in perceiving truths, while the intellect of others is not independent and is in need of teaching. As for the second: because that either follows for one who says that action is absolutely irrelevant and the statement of the teacher alone is relied upon for knowledge; but as for one who says that intellect is necessary but insufficient, rather there must be with it another teacher who guides us to the proofs and stops us at the answer to the ambiguities, that does not follow. Because we say: our intellects are not independent in knowing the proofs and answers to ambiguities, so there must be an imam who teaches us those proofs and answers, so that we, through his teaching and the strength of our intellect, know those truths. And among those truths is that he teaches us what indicates his imamate; on this estimation, circularity and infinite regress do not follow. And they argued that we see continuous disagreement among the people of the world, and if the intellect sufficed, it would not be so. And we see that a person alone cannot acquire the weakest sciences; rather he must have a teacher who guides him; that indicates that intellect is insufficient. The answer to the first: that one who engages in reflection in the mentioned manner, he does not know what you mentioned. And to the second: that there is no dispute about the [need], but the impossibility is denied; otherwise it would lead to... Then we demand them to specify that imam and show that he is from the most ignorant of people.)
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a mas'ala (question) concerning whether a teacher is needed to know God, contrary to the atheists. Razi begins by stating his proof that knowledge of the world's contingency and the need for an effecter suffices regardless of a teacher. He then presents the two arguments used by the majority (of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) to refute the atheist position: (1) if knowledge requires a teacher, then the teacher's own knowledge requires another teacher ad infinitum; (2) if knowledge of God depends on a teacher's statement, we would need prior knowledge of God to verify the teacher's truthfulness, leading to circularity. Razi then declares these two arguments weak in his view. For the first, he notes that the imam's intellect may be inherently more perfect, so he does not need a teacher. For the second, he distinguishes between those who rely solely on the teacher's word and those who accept the intellect's role but require a teacher for guidance; the latter position avoids circularity because the teacher assists the intellect without being the sole source. He then addresses objections: (a) the prevalence of disagreement shows intellect's insufficiency, but he answers that proper reflection eliminates disagreement; (b) the need for a teacher even for basic sciences is conceded, but the impossibility of intellect alone is not admitted without proof. Finally, he notes the demand to specify the imam is not binding on the opponents.

معرفة الله

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi denies all need for teachers in acquiring knowledge of God.
Correction: Razi argues that knowledge of God can be attained without a teacher in principle, but acknowledges the practical need for guidance; his critique targets the claim that a teacher is absolutely necessary.

Checkpoint
What are the two arguments Razi attributes to the majority for refuting the atheist claim that a teacher is needed to know God?
A) Infinite regress and circularity
B) Contingency and creation
C) Scripture and consensus
D) Intuition and experience

Key Takeaway
Razi's direct proof shows that knowledge of God does not require a teacher, and the majority's two arguments (infinite regress and circularity) are presented but considered weak by Razi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
What if you had to defend the existence of God using only reason, without quoting any teacher – do you have a clear argument ready in your mind? If not, what will you do to prepare?

Razi's Critique of the Two Arguments
Razi critiques the two majority arguments with alternative explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's criticisms of the two majority arguments—specifically his response to the infinite regress argument via the imam's superior intellect, and his solution to the circularity argument by distinguishing between reliance on the teacher and guidance.

Core Idea
Razi finds the majority's arguments weak and offers nuanced alternatives that avoid infinite regress and circularity.

After presenting the two arguments often used to refute the atheist claim, Razi declares them weak and proceeds to explain why. In this lesson, we examine his critique of the first argument (infinite regress) by suggesting that the imam's intellect may be inherently superior, and his critique of the second argument (circularity) by distinguishing between absolute reliance on the teacher and using the teacher as a guide while the intellect still plays a role.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's critique of the first argument
Read how Razi responds to the infinite regress argument. He suggests that the imam's intellect might be more complete, so he does not need a teacher.
Consider the possibility that some people have superior intellects.
2
Read Razi's critique of the second argument
Read his response to the circularity argument. He distinguishes between those who rely solely on the teacher's word and those who use the teacher as a guide while still using their own intellect.
The latter view avoids circularity because the intellect plays a role.
3
Compare the two critiques
Notice that Razi does not completely reject the use of teachers; rather, he refines the role they play. The imam's intellect is independent, but others need guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objections to the Intellect Sufficing and the Demand for an Imam
Razi answers two objections and issues a final demand to the atheists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atheists' objections—the persistence of disagreement and the need for teachers in basic sciences—and Razi's responses, as well as his final challenge to the atheists to specify their imam.

Core Idea
Razi addresses objections that intellect alone is insufficient and challenges the atheists to produce their imam.

In the final part of this passage, Razi turns to two objections raised by the atheists against the sufficiency of the intellect: the widespread disagreement among people, and the obvious need for teachers even in simple sciences. He offers concise answers and then issues a challenge to the atheists to specify their supposed imam and prove his authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections from the atheists
Find the objections: (1) disagreement persists among people, so intellect alone cannot be sufficient; (2) even basic sciences require a teacher, so intellect is not enough.
2
Study Razi's response to the first objection
Razi says that those who engage in proper reflection in the mentioned manner do not have such disagreement.
He implies that proper method eliminates disagreement.
3
Study Razi's response to the second objection
Razi says there is no dispute about the need for teachers (in practice), but the impossibility of the intellect alone is not conceded; the opponent's argument would lead to infinite regress.
The text here is somewhat truncated but the idea is clear.
4
Notice the final challenge
Razi demands that the atheists specify their imam and show that he is from the most ignorant people, turning the tables on them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا حاجة في معرفة الله تعالى وتقدس إلى المعلم خلافا للملاحدة لعنهم الله تعالى . لنا أنه متى حصل العلم بأن العالم ممكن وكل ممكن فله مؤثر علمنا أن العالم له مؤثر سواء كان هناك معلم أم لا ، واعتمد الجمهور منا ومن المعتزلة في إبطال قولهم على أمرين. أحدهما : أن حصول العلم بالشيء إذا افتقر إلى المعلم لافتقار علمه بكونه معلما إلى معلم آخر ولزم التسلسل . والثانى : أنا لا نعلم كون المعلم صادقا إلا بعد العلم بأن الله تعالى صدقه بواسطة إظهار المعجزة على يده ، فلو توقف العلم بالله سبحانه وتعالى على قوله لزم الدور ، فهذان الوجهان ضعيفان عندى . أما الأول : فلاحتمال أن يكون عقل الذي والإمام أكمل من عقول سائر الناس ، فلا جرم كان عقله مستقلا بإدراك الحقائق وعقل غيره لم يكن مستقل وكان محتاجا إلى التعليم . وأما الثاني : فلان ذلك إما يلزم على من يقول العمل معزول مطلقا وقول المعلم وحده معتمد للعلم ، أما من يقول العقل لابد منه لكنه غير كاف بل لا بد معه من معلم آخر يرشدنا إلى الأدلة ويوقفنا على الجواب في الشبهات، ولا يلزم منه ذلك لانا نقول : عقولنا غير مستقلة بمعرفة الدلائل والجواب عن الشبهات، فلا بد من إمام يعلمنا تلك الأدلة والأجوبة حتى أنا بواسطة تعليمه وقوة عقلنا نعرف تلك الحقائق ، ومن جملة تلك الحقائق أن يعلمنا ما يدل على إمامته وعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم الدور والتسلسل . واحتجوا بأنا نرى الاختلاف مستمرا بين أهل العالم ولو كفى العقل لما كان كذلك . ونرى أن الإنسان وحده لا يستقل بتحصيل أضعف العلوم بل لا بد له من أستاذ يهديه ، وذلك يدل على أن العقل غير كاف . والجواب عن الأول : أن من أتى بالنظر على الوجه المذكور لا يعرف له ما ذكرت . وعن الثاني أنه لا نزاع في السر لكن الامتناع ممنوع وإلا لزم ال ثم إنا نطالبهم بتعين ذلك الإمام وتبين أنه من أجهل الناس ) .
Question: There is no need in knowing God, Exalted and Sanctified, for a teacher, contrary to the atheists, may God curse them. Our proof: when knowledge is obtained that the world is contingent and every contingent has an effecter, we know that the world has an effecter whether there is a teacher or not. And the majority of us and of the Mu'tazila relied in refuting their statement on two matters. First: that obtaining knowledge of a thing, if it depends on a teacher, then the teacher's knowledge of being a teacher depends on another teacher, leading to infinite regress. Second: we do not know that the teacher is truthful except after knowing that God, Exalted, has verified him through the manifestation of a miracle at his hand; so if knowledge of God, Glorified and Exalted, depended on his statement, circularity would follow. These two aspects are weak in my view. As for the first: because it is possible that the intellect of the one who is the imam is more complete than the intellects of other people; thus his intellect is independent in perceiving truths, while the intellect of others is not independent and is in need of teaching. As for the second: because that either follows for one who says that action is absolutely irrelevant and the statement of the teacher alone is relied upon for knowledge; but as for one who says that intellect is necessary but insufficient, rather there must be with it another teacher who guides us to the proofs and stops us at the answer to the ambiguities, that does not follow. Because we say: our intellects are not independent in knowing the proofs and answers to ambiguities, so there must be an imam who teaches us those proofs and answers, so that we, through his teaching and the strength of our intellect, know those truths. And among those truths is that he teaches us what indicates his imamate; on this estimation, circularity and infinite regress do not follow. And they argued that we see continuous disagreement among the people of the world, and if the intellect sufficed, it would not be so. And we see that a person alone cannot acquire the weakest sciences; rather he must have a teacher who guides him; that indicates that intellect is insufficient. The answer to the first: that one who engages in reflection in the mentioned manner, he does not know what you mentioned. And to the second: that there is no dispute about the [need], but the impossibility is denied; otherwise it would lead to... Then we demand them to specify that imam and show that he is from the most ignorant of people.)
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a mas'ala (question) concerning whether a teacher is needed to know God, contrary to the atheists. Razi begins by stating his proof that knowledge of the world's contingency and the need for an effecter suffices regardless of a teacher. He then presents the two arguments used by the majority (of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) to refute the atheist position: (1) if knowledge requires a teacher, then the teacher's own knowledge requires another teacher ad infinitum; (2) if knowledge of God depends on a teacher's statement, we would need prior knowledge of God to verify the teacher's truthfulness, leading to circularity. Razi then declares these two arguments weak in his view. For the first, he notes that the imam's intellect may be inherently more perfect, so he does not need a teacher. For the second, he distinguishes between those who rely solely on the teacher's word and those who accept the intellect's role but require a teacher for guidance; the latter position avoids circularity because the teacher assists the intellect without being the sole source. He then addresses objections: (a) the prevalence of disagreement shows intellect's insufficiency, but he answers that proper reflection eliminates disagreement; (b) the need for a teacher even for basic sciences is conceded, but the impossibility of intellect alone is not admitted without proof. Finally, he notes the demand to specify the imam is not binding on the opponents.

عقل
أستاذ
إمام
نظر

A) Proper reflection removes disagreement
B) Disagreement is inevitable
C) Intellect is not the only source
D) Teachers cause disagreement

Key Takeaway
The atheists' objections are refuted by pointing to proper reflection and denying the absolute impossibility of intellect alone, and the demand for an imam challenges the atheists' position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face disagreement in religious matters, do you default to skepticism about reason, or do you, like Razi, demand that the disputants examine their method of reflection? Where do you need to refine your own reflective process?
?
Razi demands that the atheists name their imam. Who is the ultimate authority in your life? Is it a scholar, a book, or your own intellect? Are you prepared to defend that choice with evidence?
?
If the weakest science requires a teacher, how do you seek guidance in matters of faith while still affirming the sufficiency of intellect? In what areas do you rely too heavily on others without exercising your own reasoning?
