The Objection That Wujub Is Not a Positive Attribute
Objector argues wujub is not positive; author criticizes his escape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that wujub is not a positive attribute, and the author's critique of the objector's escape to homonymy.

Core Idea
The objector claims wujub is not positive, but his argument and escape are flawed.

After the initial claim, Razi introduces an objector who argues that wujub (necessity) is not a positive attribute (wasf thubuti). The objector uses the same homonymy dilemma to conclude that existence is not a positive attribute. Razi then criticizes the objector's eventual escape to homonymy, showing that it does not resolve the confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's argument
The objector argues that if wujub were a positive attribute, it would have to be predicated of both types either univocally or equivocally, both of which are invalid. Therefore, wujub is not positive.
Note the objector's reliance on the earlier argument about homonymy.
2
Understand the author's response
Razi rebuts by pointing out that the objector's escape to homonymy as a 'refuge' does not save him from perplexity, and he criticizes the objector's inconsistency.
Razi uses strong language: 'extreme confusion' and 'does not care about contradiction'.
3
Reflect on the dialectic
This section shows the back-and-forth in kalam arguments: an objector raises a point, the author responds, and then the author criticizes the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : وقوع لفظ الواجب على الواجب بالذات والواجب بالغير بالاشتراك اللفظى . وإلا فالوجوب بالذات مركب فيكون ممكنا ولأن القدر المشترك إن كان غنيا عن الغير لم يكن تمام ماهية الوجوب بالغير عارضا للغير هذا خلف، وإن كان مفتقرا لم يكن تمام ماهية الوجوب بالذات غنيا عن الغير. فمورض بأن مسمى الوجوب يمكن تقسيمه إلى الواجب بالذات وإلى الواجب بالغير ومورد التقسيم مشترك بين القسمين لا محالة. والقائل أن يستدل على أن الوجوب ليس وصفا ثبوتيا بأنه لو كان وصفا ثبوتيا لمكان إما أن يكون مقولا على الواجب بالذات والواجب بالغير بالاشتراك المعنوى أو بالاشتراك اللفظ وهما باطلان على ما تقدم فالوجود ليس وصفا ثبوتيا. والمعارضة بكون الواجب مساويا للممكن فى الوجود فقد بينا أن اشتراكهما في الوجود ليس بالتواطؤ. والمهرب الذى هرب إليه أخيرا إن الوجوب بالذات مقول على الواجبين بالاشتراك اللفظى لا ينجيه هذه الحيرة، وإنه من غاية التحير لا يدرى إلى أى شىء يتأدى كلامه ولا يبالى بالتناقض ولا الإلزام مالا يخلصه من حيرته. وكان من الواجب أن يقول كما قال غيره من الحكماء : الواجب لذاته يستحيل أن يكون محمولا على اثنين لأنه إما أن يكون ذاتيا لهما أو عرضيا لهما أو ذاتيا لأحدهما عرضيا للآخر. فإن كان ذاتيا لهما فالخصوصية التي بها يمتاز كل واحد من الآخر لا يمكن أن يكون داخلا في المعنى المشترك. وإلا فلا امتياز فهو خارج فينضاف إلى المعنى المشترك. فإن كان فى كل واحدة منهما كان كل واحدة منهما ممكنة من حيث هو موجود وممتاز من الآخر. وإن كان فى أحدهما فهو ممكن. وإن كان عرضا لهما أو لأحدهما فمعروضه لا يكون واجبا. لا يقال الواجب المعنى المشترك فقط لأنا بينا أن المعنى المشترك لا يوجد في الخارج من حيث هو مشترك من غير تخصيص يزيل اشتراكه. فإن قيل المخصص سلب وكل واحد منهما مختص بأنه ليس الآخر. قلنا سلب الغير لا يتحصل إلا بعد حصول الغير وحينئذ يكون كل واحد هو هو بعد حصول الغير فيكون ممكنا. وفيه كفاية في هذا المطلوب . أقول : لا يلزم من كون الوجوب مشتركا بين الوجوب بالذات والوجوب بالغير كون الوجوب بالذات لا يفتقر إلى تعقل غير الذات، أما الوجوب بالغير فيفتقر تعقله إلى انضياف تعقل الغير وإلى تعقل الوجوب. ثم لو كان الوجوب الذى هو أمر يحصل في العقل عند إسناد متصور إلى الوجود الخارجى مركبا لم يلزم منه تركيب المسند إليه. كما لا يلزم من كونه محتاجا إلى موصوف به كون الموصوف به محتاجا إلى غيره. وأيضاً الامتناع أيضاً مشترك بين الامتناع بالذات والامتناع بالغير، ولا يجب من تركيبه تركب في الممتنع لذاته الذى يكون منعا محضا. وقوله في الوجه الثانى : القدر المشترك إن كان غنيا من الغير لم يكن تمام ماهية الوجوب بالغير عارضا للغير هذا خلف. فيه نظر، لأنه لا يلزم منه الخلف، فإن من استغناء الجزء لا يلزم استغناء المركب، بل إنما يلزم من افتقار الجزء افتقار المركب. والمعارضة التي أوردها حجة على الاشتراك المعنوى في الوجوب. واستدلاله على كون الوجوب غير ثبوتى باطل كما مر.
Issue: The occurrence of the word 'wajib' upon the necessary by essence and the necessary by other is by homonymy. Otherwise, the necessity by essence would be composite and thus possible. And because the common factor, if it were independent of the other, would not be the entire quiddity of the necessity by other being an accident of the other—this is a contradiction. And if it were dependent, it would not be the entire quiddity of the necessity by essence independent of the other. So it is objected that the named 'necessity' can be divided into the necessary by essence and the necessary by other, and the subject of division is shared between the two divisions necessarily. And the objector argues that necessity is not a positive attribute because if it were a positive attribute, then it would be predicated of the necessary by essence and the necessary by other either by univocal homonymy or by equivocal homonymy, and both are invalid as previously mentioned. So existence is not a positive attribute. And the objection that the necessary is equal to the possible in existence—we have shown that their sharing in existence is not by univocality. The escape which he finally escapes to, that necessity by essence is predicated of the two necessaries by equivocal homonymy, does not save him from this perplexity. He is in extreme confusion: he does not know to what his speech leads, nor does he care about contradiction or implication that does not release him from his confusion. He ought to have said, as other philosophers said: The necessary by essence cannot be predicated of two, because it is either essential for both, accidental for both, or essential for one and accidental for the other. If it is essential for both, then the particularity by which each is distinguished from the other cannot be included in the common meaning; otherwise there would be no distinction. So it is outside and is added to the common meaning. If it is in each of them, then each of them is possible insofar as it exists and is distinguished from the other. If it is in one of them, then it is possible. If it is accidental for both or for one, then its subject cannot be necessary. It cannot be said that the necessary is only the common meaning, because we have shown that the common meaning does not exist externally insofar as it is common without a specification removing its commonality. If it is said: The specification is negation, and each one is specified by not being the other. We say: The negation of the other is only obtained after the obtaining of the other. Then each one is itself after the obtaining of the other, and thus it is possible. And in this is sufficiency for this purpose. I say: From the fact that necessity is shared between necessity by essence and necessity by other, it does not follow that necessity by essence does not require conceptualization of something other than the essence. As for necessity by other, its conceptualization requires adding conceptualization of the other and conceptualization of necessity. Furthermore, if necessity, which is something that occurs in the mind when a conceived thing is attributed to external existence, were composite, it would not follow from that composition in the subject of attribution. Just as, from its being needful of something described by it, it does not follow that the described thing is needful of something else. Also, impossibility is also shared between impossibility by essence and impossibility by other, and its composition does not necessitate composition in the impossible by essence, which is pure negation. And his statement in the second aspect: 'The common factor, if it were independent of the other, would not be the entire quiddity of the necessity by other being an accident of the other—this is a contradiction.' There is consideration in it, because the contradiction does not follow from it. For from the independence of the part, the independence of the composite does not follow; rather, from the dependence of the part, the dependence of the composite follows. And the objection he raised is an argument for univocal homonymy in necessity. And his inference that necessity is not positive is invalid as previously mentioned.

Commentary
This passage presents a complex issue about the term 'wajib' (necessary) and whether it applies univocally or equivocally to necessary by essence (wājib bi al-dhāt) and necessary by other (wājib bi al-ghayr). The author begins by stating that the word is used by homonymy (al-ishtirāk al-lafẓī). He then argues that if it were univocal, necessity by essence would be composite, leading to impossibility. The author then criticizes this objector's escape to equivocal homonymy as insufficient, and presents the correct view of the philosophers: the necessary by essence cannot be predicated of two, and he provides a logical analysis based on essential and accidental predication. The passage concludes with the author's own commentary ('aqūl) clarifying that the shared nature of necessity does not entail composition in the necessary by essence, and that the objector's inference that necessity is not positive is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Philosophers' Correct View and the Author's Clarification
Philosophers' analysis of predication and author's clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' correct analysis that the necessary by essence cannot be predicated of two, and the author's clarification that shared necessity does not entail composition in the necessary by essence.

Core Idea
The necessary by essence cannot be a predicate for two distinct subjects.

Having criticized the objector, Razi now presents the correct view as held by other philosophers (al-hukama'): the necessary by essence cannot be predicated of two. He provides a detailed logical analysis based on essential and accidental predication. Then, in his own voice ('aqulu), he clarifies that shared necessity does not cause composition in the necessary by essence, and addresses the objector's second argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' rule and its subdivisions
The philosophers state that necessary by essence cannot be predicated of two, because it would have to be either essential for both, accidental for both, or essential for one and accidental for the other. Each case is examined.
This is a rigorous logical classification.
2
Understand the argument about particularity
If the predicate were essential for both, the particularity distinguishing each must be external; if it is in each, they become possible; if accidental, the subject cannot be necessary.
The key is the distinction between common meaning and particularizing factor.
3
Examine the author's own commentary
Razi adds that shared necessity does not make the necessary by essence need something else, and composition in the concept does not imply composition in the subject. He also critiques the objector's second argument.
The author defends the positive attribute view.

Issue: The occurrence of the word 'wajib' upon the necessary by essence and the necessary by other is by homonymy. Otherwise, the necessity by essence would be composite and thus possible. And because the common factor, if it were independent of the other, would not be the entire quiddity of the necessity by other being an accident of the other—this is a contradiction. And if it were dependent, it would not be the entire quiddity of the necessity by essence independent of the other. So it is objected that the named 'necessity' can be divided into the necessary by essence and the necessary by other, and the subject of division is shared between the two divisions necessarily. And the objector argues that necessity is not a positive attribute because if it were a positive attribute, then it would be predicated of the necessary by essence and the necessary by other either by univocal homonymy or by equivocal homonymy, and both are invalid as previously mentioned. So existence is not a positive attribute. And the objection that the necessary is equal to the possible in existence—we have shown that their sharing in existence is not by univocality. The escape which he finally escapes to, that necessity by essence is predicated of the two necessaries by equivocal homonymy, does not save him from this perplexity. He is in extreme confusion: he does not know to what his speech leads, nor does he care about contradiction or implication that does not release him from his confusion. He ought to have said, as other philosophers said: The necessary by essence cannot be predicated of two, because it is either essential for both, accidental for both, or essential for one and accidental for the other. If it is essential for both, then the particularity by which each is distinguished from the other cannot be included in the common meaning; otherwise there would be no distinction. So it is outside and is added to the common meaning. If it is in each of them, then each of them is possible insofar as it exists and is distinguished from the other. If it is in one of them, then it is possible. If it is accidental for both or for one, then its subject cannot be necessary. It cannot be said that the necessary is only the common meaning, because we have shown that the common meaning does not exist externally insofar as it is common without a specification removing its commonality. If it is said: The specification is negation, and each one is specified by not being the other. We say: The negation of the other is only obtained after the obtaining of the other. Then each one is itself after the obtaining of the other, and thus it is possible. And in this is sufficiency for this purpose. I say: From the fact that necessity is shared between necessity by essence and necessity by other, it does not follow that necessity by essence does not require conceptualization of something other than the essence. As for necessity by other, its conceptualization requires adding conceptualization of the other and conceptualization of necessity. Furthermore, if necessity, which is something that occurs in the mind when a conceived thing is attributed to external existence, were composite, it would not follow from that composition in the subject of attribution. Just as, from its being needful of something described by it, it does not follow that the described thing is needful of something else. Also, impossibility is also shared between impossibility by essence and impossibility by other, and its composition does not necessitate composition in the impossible by essence, which is pure negation. And his statement in the second aspect: 'The common factor, if it were independent of the other, would not be the entire quiddity of the necessity by other being an accident of the other—this is a contradiction.' There is consideration in it, because the contradiction does not follow from it. For from the independence of the part, the independence of the composite does not follow; rather, from the dependence of the part, the dependence of the composite follows. And the objection he raised is an argument for univocal homonymy in necessity. And his inference that necessity is not positive is invalid as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a complex issue about the term 'wajib' (necessary) and whether it applies univocally or equivocally to necessary by essence (wājib bi al-dhāt) and necessary by other (wājib bi al-ghayr). The author begins by stating that the word is used by homonymy (al-ishtirāk al-lafẓī). He then argues that if it were univocal, necessity by essence would be composite, leading to impossibility. The argument proceeds through a dialectic: a common factor (al-qadr al-mushtarak) is considered; if independent, it cannot be the entire quiddity of necessity by other; if dependent, it cannot be the entire quiddity of necessity by essence. An objector (al-qā'il) argues that necessity is not a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī) because if it were, it would be predicated of both either univocally or equivocally, both invalid. The author then criticizes this objector's escape to equivocal homonymy as insufficient, and presents the correct view of the philosophers: the necessary by essence cannot be predicated of two, and he provides a logical analysis based on essential and accidental predication. The passage concludes with the author's own commentary ('aqūl) clarifying that the shared nature of necessity does not entail composition in the necessary by essence, and that the objector's inference that necessity is not positive is invalid.

Key Terms
ذاتي
عرضي
قدر مشترك
واجب بالذات
وجوب

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can the necessary by essence not be predicated of two?
A) Because it is too great to be shared.
B) Because it would lead to either essential or accidental predication, both of which are impossible.
C) Because the two types are completely different.
D) Because there is no common meaning.

Key Takeaway
The necessary by essence cannot be a predicate for two distinct subjects; shared necessity does not cause composition in the necessary by essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the necessary by essence cannot be predicated of two, then your understanding of Allah's oneness must be absolute. Have you ever attributed to Allah a quality that implied composition or need? Pledge now to study the divine attributes with this standard.
?
The philosophers' argument forces you to see that any commonality between Allah and creation leads to contingency. Where in your life have you treated Allah's attributes as similar to human traits? Repent and commit to learning the uniquely divine meanings of His names.

The Necessary by Essence Cannot Be Annihilated
Razi gives a proof that the necessary by essence cannot be annihilated and comments on it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that the necessary by essence cannot undergo annihilation and why the impossibility is by essence, not due to an external factor.

Core Idea
The necessary cannot cease to exist because its existence is essential, not dependent on anything external.

This lesson explores a foundational argument in Islamic theology: the impossibility of annihilation for God. The author presents a brief proof and then refines it, emphasizing that the necessity is intrinsic, not derived from external causes. Let's unpack the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: claim, proof, objection, response.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'necessary by essence' (واجب لذاته), 'possible by essence' (ممكن بالذات), and 'impossible by essence' (ممتنع لذاته). Understand their definitions.
These terms are central to the proof.
3
Analyze the initial proof and objection
The objection says if annihilation were possible, existence would depend on absence of a cause of non-existence, making it contingent. Identify why this reasoning is flawed according to the author.
4
Understand the author's correction
The author states that non-existence is impossible by essence, not due to an external cause. See how he refutes the earlier reasoning as analyzing essence with an external cause.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this argument matters: it establishes God's absolute necessity and independence, a cornerstone of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الواجب لذاته لا يصح عليه العدم . إذ لو صح لكان وجوده متوقفا على عدم سبب عدمه، والمتوقف على الغير ممكن بالذات . أقول : الجواب فيه أن يقال لا يصح عليه العدم لأن وجوده واجب لذاته. وما ذكره ليس بصواب لأن عدم واجب الوجود ممتنع لذاته لا لغيره. وتعليله بعدم توقف وجوده على عدم سبب عدمه تحليل ماهية الشيء لذاته بعلة غير ذاته.
Issue: The necessary by essence cannot undergo annihilation. For if it were possible, its existence would depend on the non-existence of a cause for its non-existence, and what depends on something else is possible by essence. I say: The answer to it is that it cannot undergo annihilation because its existence is necessary by essence. And what he mentioned is not correct because the non-existence of the Necessary Existence is impossible by essence, not because of something else. And his explanation that its existence does not depend on the non-existence of a cause for its non-existence is an analysis of the quiddity of the thing by itself with a cause other than itself.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument about the impossibility of annihilation for the necessary by essence. The objection is: if annihilation were possible, then the existence of the necessary would depend on the non-existence of a cause that would annihilate it, and what depends on something else is possible. The author then gives his commentary ('aqūl): the correct answer is that annihilation is impossible because its existence is necessary by essence, not because of any external factor. The non-existence of the Necessary Existence is inherently impossible. He criticizes the initial reasoning as analyzing the quiddity of a thing by itself with a cause other than itself, implying it is flawed because it treats the necessary as dependent on an external cause.

The Contingent Requires a Separate Cause
Imam al-Razi states the issue and gives a brief proof from equal possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam principle that a contingent thing requires a separate cause for its existence or non-existence, and the proof from equal possibility.

Core Idea
A contingent thing cannot tip the balance between existence and non-existence without an external cause.

Imam al-Razi presents a foundational principle in Islamic theology: everything that can either exist or not exist needs an external factor to make it exist. This principle is the cornerstone of the kalam cosmological argument. Let's see how he proves it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise style: the issue is stated, then a brief proof follows.
The proof is only one sentence, so every word matters.
2
Identify key terms
Look for the terms: الممكن (the contingent), السبب المنفصل (separate cause), الترجيح (preponderance), and المرجح (preponderator). Understand their meanings.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the proof logic
The proof states: existence and non-existence are equally possible for a contingent. Therefore, for one to become actual, an external factor must tip the balance. This is the principle of 'preponderance requires a preponderator'.
This is a self-evident rational principle used to argue for a necessary being.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this principle is important for proving the existence of a Creator. How does it lead to the necessity of a cause outside the universe?
This principle is used in the kalam cosmological argument to show the universe requires a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الممكن لا يوجد ولا يعدم إلا بسبب منفصل . لأنهما لما استويا بالنسبة إليه استمال الترجيح إلا المنفصل .
Issue: The contingent does not exist and does not cease to exist except by a separate cause. Because when the two [existence and non-existence] are equal with respect to it, preponderance [of one over the other] is [achieved] only by the separate [cause].
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage states the fundamental issue (mas'ala) that a contingent thing (al-mumkin) cannot come into existence or cease to exist except through a separate cause (sabab munfasil). The proof is given: existence and non-existence are equally possible for the contingent; therefore, a preponderance (tarjih) of one side requires an external preponderator (murjih). This is a foundational principle in Ilm al-Kalam for proving the need for a Creator.

Key Terms
الممكن
السبب المنفصل
الترجيح
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof is circular.
Correction: It relies on the self-evident principle of the need for a preponderator, which is not circular but a foundational axiom.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the contingent need a separate cause?
A) Because existence is impossible without a cause.
B) Because existence and non-existence are equally possible, so preponderance requires an external factor.
C) Because the contingent is inherently dependent.
D) Because the mind cannot conceive a self-caused being.

Key Takeaway
A contingent thing's existence requires an external cause because existence and non-existence are equally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if your existence depends on Allah every moment, or do you slip into a sense of self-sufficiency? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself of your contingency?
?
When you look at your own life, do you see everything as equally possible without Allah's will? How would your reliance on Him change if you internalized that every breath is a gift from the external Cause?
?
The proof shows that even your ceasing to exist (death) requires a cause. Are you prepared to meet your Maker? What changes in your priorities if you take this seriously?

Critique of the Proof for Necessary Attributes
Imam Razi critiques the proof for the necessity of the possible and declares it superfluous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi considers the proof for the necessity of the possible to be a superfluous restatement of a self-evident principle.

Core Idea
The so-called proof for necessity merely repackages a self-evident truth.

Imam Razi examines a common proof that claims necessity is a real attribute of the possible. He points out that the proof's key premise is already contained in a self-evident judgment. This lesson unpacks his critique and shows why the proof is unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi first states the nature of necessity as mental, then critiques the proof.
Pay attention to his use of 'self-evident judgment' (ḥukm badīhī).
2
Identify the two key claims
Razi makes two points: (1) necessity is a mental attribute, (2) the proof is based on a premise that is itself self-evident. Identify these in the text.
3
Understand the critique
Razi argues that the proof's premise 'the possible, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist' only holds if you already know that every effect has a cause. This is the same as the principle of preponderance.
So the proof adds nothing new.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the demonstration is superfluous. Ask yourself: What does this mean for the overall argument about divine attributes?
This critique shows that some theological arguments may be circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق : أن ذلك الوجوب أمر عقلى كسائر الصفات ويسكون قائما بالتصور من الممكن عند الحكم بحدوثه وأقول من رأى أن البرهان الذي أقامه مبنى على حكم هو قول الممسكن ما لم يجب لم يوجد وهذا انضية لا يصح الحكم فيها إلا إذا علم أن كل مسبب فله سبب ، وفي قولنا ترجح أحد المتساويين يحتاج إلى مرجح هذا المعنى بعينه موجود ولكن بعبارة أخرى فإذا البرهان الذي أقامه مبنى على ما يتضمنه الحكم البديهى المذكور الذي عدل عنه إلى ذلك البرهان ، فقد وضح من ذلك أن ذلك البرهان فضلة غير محتاج إليه.
And the truth is: that necessity (al-wujūb) is a mental matter like the rest of attributes, and it subsists in the mental conception of the possible (al-mumkin) when judging its temporal origination. And I say: whoever sees that the demonstration he constructed is based on a judgment which is the statement: 'the possible, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist' — but this is a proposition in which judgment is not valid unless it is known that every effect has a cause. And in our statement: 'the preponderance of one of the two equals requires a preponderator' — this same meaning is present but in another expression. Thus the demonstration he constructed is based on what is implied by the aforementioned self-evident judgment from which he turned away to that demonstration. So it is clear from that that that demonstration is a superfluity, not needed.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a proof for the necessity of the possible (wujūb al-mumkin). Imam Razi states that the claimed necessity is actually a mental attribute (amr ʿaqlī) like other attributes, subsisting in the mind when we conceive the possible and judge its temporal origination. He then points out that the proof the opponent builds relies on a premise: 'the possible, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist.' But this premise itself only holds if one already knows that every effect has a cause — which is a self-evident judgment. The same self-evident judgment is also contained in the well-known principle 'the preponderance of one of the two equals requires a preponderator.' Therefore, the opponent's elaborate proof merely restates the self-evident principle in different words. Hence, the proof is unnecessary (faḍla ghayr muḥtāj ilayh). The critique follows the author's argumentative order, first identifying the mental nature of necessity, then exposing the redundancy of the proof.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الممكن
أمر عقلي
حكم بديهي
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that necessity is a real attribute subsisting in the possible outside the mind.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that necessity is a mental attribute (amr ʿaqlī), not a real external attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why is the proof for the necessity of the possible superfluous?
A) Because the necessity is a real attribute and known a priori.
B) Because the proof restates a self-evident judgment that already implies the same conclusion.
C) Because the possible does not require a cause.
D) Because the proof is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi exposes that the proof for the necessity of the possible is merely a restatement of a self-evident principle and thus unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you content with the simple, self-evident truths of faith, or do you seek complex arguments to feel intellectually superior? What does your preference reveal about your heart?
?
When you engage in theological discussions, do you ever catch yourself relying on elaborate proofs that merely restate what you already know deep down? How might that be a form of pride?
?
If the most fundamental truth about God's existence is self-evident, what excuses have you been hiding behind to avoid responding to it with your whole being?

The Possible in Itself is Equal in Both Sides
Al-Razi proves that a possible thing is inherently balanced between existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi argues that a possible thing is inherently neutral between existence and non-existence, and what follows from that.

Core Idea
A possible thing is inherently balanced; only a cause tips the scale.

Al-Razi addresses a fundamental question: can a possible thing have a natural inclination toward existence or non-existence? Through a careful argument, he shows that without an external cause, a possible thing must be perfectly equal in both directions. This reasoning is foundational for later discussions about the necessity of a cause for everything that occurs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the logical flow and the key terms used.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'because it is not permissible...' followed by two branches.
2
Identify the assumption and the two branches
Al-Razi assumes the opposite: that one side is preponderant. He then considers whether the other side can occur or not, leading to two branches.
The two branches cover both possibilities: (a) the other side can occur, (b) it cannot occur.
3
Follow the logical steps in each branch
In the first branch, he argues that if the other side can occur, it must be due to a cause, and the preponderance alone is insufficient. In the second, if it cannot occur, then the preponderant side becomes necessary, contradicting the assumption of possibility.
Each branch leads to a contradiction, so the initial assumption of preponderance without a cause is false.
4
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, a possible thing cannot have a preponderant side without a cause; it is inherently equal (mutasawi al-tarafayn).
This conclusion is essential for the principle that everything contingent requires a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الممكن لذاته متساوى الطرفين. لأنه لا يجوز أن يكون أحد طرفيه أولى من الآخر، لأنه مع تلك الأولوية إما أن يمكن طريان الطرف الآخر أو لا يمكن. فإن أمكن فإما أن يكون طريانه لسبب أو لا لسبب. فإن كان لسبب لم تكن تلك الأولوية كافية في بقاء الطرف الراجح بل لابد معها من عدم سبب الطرف المرجوح. وإن كان لا لسبب فقد وقع الممكن الموجود لا لعلة وهذا محال. لأن أحد القسمين أقوى من المرجوح، فلما امتنع الوقوع حال التساوى فلأن يمتنع حال المرجوحية كان ذلك أولى. وإن لم يمكن طريان المرجوح كان الراجح واجباً والمرجوح ممتنعاً.
Issue: The possible in itself is equal in its two sides. Because it is not permissible that one of its two sides be more preponderant than the other. For with that preponderance, either the occurrence of the other side is possible or not. If possible, then either its occurrence is due to a cause or not due to a cause. If due to a cause, then that preponderance is not sufficient for the persistence of the preponderant side; rather, along with it there must be the absence of the cause of the preponderated side. And if not due to a cause, then a possible existent has occurred without a cause, and that is impossible. For one of the two divisions is stronger than the preponderated [side]. Since occurrence is impossible in the state of equality, it is even more impossible in the state of being preponderated. And if the occurrence of the preponderated side is not possible, then the preponderant side is necessary and the preponderated side is impossible.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a possible thing (mumkin li-dhatihi) is inherently equal in its two sides (existence and non-existence). Al-Razi argues by assuming the opposite: if one side were preponderant (rajih) without a cause, we consider whether the other side can occur. If it can occur, it must be due to a cause (otherwise it would be uncaused, which is impossible). If due to a cause, then the initial preponderance alone is insufficient to maintain the preponderant side—the absence of the cause of the other side is also needed. If the other side cannot occur, then the preponderant side is necessary and the other impossible, contradicting the assumption that the thing is possible. Therefore, in a possible thing, neither side can be preponderant without a cause; hence it is equal (mutasawi al-tarafayn).

Key Terms
ممكن لذاته
متساوى الطرفين
راجح
مرجوح
علة
وجوب
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A possible thing can have a natural inclination toward existence or non-existence without an external cause.
Correction: Al-Razi proves that without a cause, a possible thing must be perfectly neutral; any preponderance requires a cause.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Razi conclude about a possible thing in itself?
A) It tends towards existence.
B) It is equal in its two sides of existence and non-existence.
C) It always needs a cause for existence but not for non-existence.
D) It is impossible for it to be preponderated.

Key Takeaway
For a possible thing, without an external cause, its existence and non-existence are perfectly equal; any preponderance requires a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are, in yourself, a possible being—no more likely to exist than not. Yet you exist. What external cause keeps you from non-existence right now? How does that realization change the way you approach your Creator today? Commit to one act that acknowledges your absolute dependence.
?
Consider something you take for granted—your health, your faith, your next breath. Without a cause, its existence is no more likely than its absence. How does this shift your gratitude? What cause have you been ignoring? What will you do to thank the One who tips the scale?
?
Al-Razi's logic strips away any illusion of self-sufficiency. Is there an area of your life where you secretly think you are 'necessary'—that you cannot be shaken? What would it mean to truly accept that you are possible, held in existence only by God's will? Write down one fear that this truth exposes, and turn it into a prayer.

The Two External Necessities of the Possible
Al-Razi explains the two necessities flanking a possible thing's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the preponderance of a possible thing requires two external necessities—one preceding and one following its existence.

Core Idea
A possible thing's existence is sandwiched between two necessities, but neither is part of its essence.

Al-Razi addresses a key point in divine existence and rational judgments: the status of possible things. He argues that the very fact that a possible thing is 'preponderated' into existence implies necessity on both sides—before and after existence—yet these necessities are not intrinsic to the possible. This lesson unpacks that subtle argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Al-Razi's own concise style come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi uses a very compressed style, typical of later kalam.
2
Identify the two necessities
The passage presents two necessities: one preceding (sabiq) and one following (lahiq). Find the reason given for each.
The preceding necessity is based on the preponderant only occurring with necessity. The following necessity is based on existence contradicting non-existence.
3
Reflect on 'external' vs 'internal'
Al-Razi emphasizes that these two necessities are 'kharijan' (external) not 'dakhilan' (internal). Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for the concept of a possible thing?
This distinction preserves the contingency of the possible in itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: رجحان الممكن لذاته مسبوق بوجوب وملحوق بوجوب. أما السابق فلأنه ما لم يترجح صدوره عن المؤثر على الصدور عنه لم يوجد، وقد دللنا على أن الراجح لا يحصل إلا مع الوجوب. وأما اللاحق فلأن وجوده ينافي عدمه فكان منافياً لإمكان عدمه فكان مستلزماً للوجوب. واعلم أن شيئاً من الممكنات لا ينفك عن هذين الوجوبين، لكنهما خارجان لا داخلان.
Issue: The preponderance of the possible in itself is preceded by necessity and followed by necessity. As for the preceding, because as long as its emanation from the influencer over not emanating from him is not preponderant, it does not exist, and we have demonstrated that the preponderant only occurs with necessity. As for the following, because its existence contradicts its non-existence, so it contradicts the possibility of its non-existence, and thus entails necessity. And know that no possible thing is free of these two necessities, but they are external to it, not internal.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage states that the preponderance (rajhan) of a possible thing is preceded by a necessity (wujub) and followed by another necessity. The preceding necessity: before the possible thing can exist, its emanation from the influencer must become preponderant over its non-emanation; this preponderance only occurs with necessity (as proven earlier that the preponderant requires necessity). The following necessity: once the possible exists, its existence contradicts its non-existence, thus it becomes necessary (impossible to be non-existent) at that moment. Al-Razi adds that these two necessities are not intrinsic to the possible (they are 'outside' it), meaning the possible in itself does not necessitate either existence or non-existence, but these necessities are acquired from the cause and from the state of existence itself.

Key Terms
رجحان
ممكن
وجوب
صدور
مؤثر
وجود
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the possible itself becomes necessary by its own essence.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that the two necessities are external to the possible, not internal, so the possible remains contingent in itself.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, why is the preponderance of the possible preceded by necessity?
A) Because the possible requires a cause that necessitates its existence.
B) Because the preponderant only occurs with necessity.
C) Because existence contradicts non-existence.
D) Because the influencer is necessary.

Key Takeaway
A possible thing is never free from two external necessities—one before and one after existence—yet remains contingent in its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence at this very moment is sandwiched between a necessity that brought you into being and a necessity that keeps you from vanishing. Do you truly live with the awareness that you are never self-sufficient—or do you act as if your next breath depends on you?
?
Al-Razi says the possible is never free of these two necessities, yet they are external to its essence. Examine your own sense of identity: how much of your self-worth is built on the illusion that you are the source of your own existence and stability?
?
If every possible thing, including you, is flanked by necessity, then your entire life—every action, every thought—depends on the One who sustains the preponderance. What would change today if you genuinely believed that you cannot take a single step without that preceding necessity?

The Cause of Need: Possibility vs. Origination
Al-Razi argues for possibility as the cause of need and presents an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that possibility (imkan) is the ontological basis for needing an influencer, not temporal origination (huduth), and be aware of the objection concerning non-existent possibles.

Core Idea
Al-Razi: It's possibility, not origination, that makes a thing need a cause.

Al-Razi tackles a fundamental question in Islamic theology: why do things need an external cause? Is it because they come into existence in time (origination) or because they are possible in themselves? In this passage, he argues that it is possibility itself, and not the fact of having a beginning in time, that grounds the need. He then presents an objection from those who argue that if possibility were the cause, even non-existent things would need causes—a problem he leaves unresolved here.

Guided Path
1
Read Al-Razi's core argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the first part where Al-Razi presents his reasoning for why possibility, not origination, is the cause of need.
Notice how he uses the idea of 'posteriority' to argue that origination cannot be the cause.
2
Understand the logical structure
Al-Razi's argument is a reductio: if origination were the cause, it would lead to a circular regression. Trace the steps: origination is a quality of existence → it is posterior to existence → existence is posterior to the agent's influence → thus the need would be posterior to itself.
This is a classic kalam argument using causality and priority.
3
Examine the objection
The objectors argue that if possibility were the cause, then non-existent possibles (ma'dum mumkin) would also need an influencer. But non-existence cannot be an effect because an effect must be something real.
Al-Razi does not answer this objection here; it is a challenge the reader must consider.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters: it shifts the focus from temporal beginning to ontological dependency. This has implications for arguments for God's existence.
This connects to the wider Kalam cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر الإمكان لا الحدوث. لأن الحدوث كيفية في وجود الحادث فيكون متأخراً عنه، والوجود متأخر عن تأثير القادر فيه، والمتأخر من احتياج الممكن إليه المتأخر من علة احتياجه إليه، فلو كانت العلة هي الحدوث لزم تأخير الشيء عن نفسه مراتب. احتجوا بأن علة الحاجة لو كانت هي الإمكان لزم احتياج العدم الممكن إلى المؤثر وهو محال، لأن المؤثر يستدعي حصول الأثر والعدم نفي محض فلا يكون أثراً.
Issue: The cause of the need for an influencer is possibility, not origination. Because origination is a modality in the existence of the originated thing, so it is posterior to it, and existence is posterior to the influence of the powerful [agent] on it, and that which is posterior to the need of the possible for it is posterior to the cause of its need for it. So if the cause were origination, there would follow a delay of the thing from itself by several degrees. They argued that if the cause of need were possibility, then the need of possible non-existence for an influencer would follow, which is impossible, because the influencer requires the occurrence of the effect, and non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot be an effect.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage establishes that the reason a possible being needs an influencer is its possibility (imkan), not its temporal origination (huduth). Al-Razi argues that origination is a quality of the existence of the originated thing, so it is posterior to that existence. Existence itself is posterior to the influence of the agent. Thus, if origination were the cause of need, the need would be posterior to the thing's existence, leading to a circular regression. The objectors raise a counterargument: if possibility is the cause, then non-existent possibles would also need an influencer, but non-existence cannot be an effect. Al-Razi is presenting this as an objection to be answered; the passage ends there, implying further discussion. The lesson here is the initial argument for possibility over origination as the ground of need.

Key Terms
علة الحاجة
مؤثر
إمكان
حدوث
وجود
عدم
أثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only things that begin in time need a cause.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the very possibility of a thing, not its temporal origin, is what makes it dependent on an external cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi reject origination (huduth) as the cause of need for an influencer?
A) Because origination is a quality of existence and thus posterior to it, leading to circularity.
B) Because possibility is a stronger criterion.
C) Because origination only applies to temporal things.
D) Because the Quran supports possibility.

Key Takeaway
The need of a possible being for an influencer is grounded in its possibility (imkan), not its temporal origination (huduth), though the objection regarding non-existent things remains to be addressed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is only possible—not necessary—then you depend at every moment on the One who gives you existence. Do you live with this constant awareness, or do you slip into feeling self-sufficient? What would change if you truly felt your possibility?
?
Al-Razi argues that your need for Allah is not because of when you were created but because of what you are: a pure possibility. If need is built into your essence, can you ever be independent of Him for even a second? How does this reshape your trust in your own plans and abilities?
?
The objection about non-existent possibles reminds us that even what is not yet created would need a cause if it were to exist. Does your heart recognize that everything—including your future, your hopes, your dreams—depends entirely on His will? Are you submitting to that reality or fighting it?

Division of Existents and Philosophers' Argument for Time
Razi classifies existents and presents the philosophers' argument for time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of existents into qadim and hadith, and the philosophical argument that God's pre-eternal existence implies time as a real entity.

Core Idea
God's pre-eternity forces a real time alongside Him, according to philosophers.

This lesson begins with the foundational Kalam classification of all existents into the eternal (qadim) and the originated (hadith). It then presents the philosophers' subtle argument: the very concept of God existing in pre-eternity must be something real and distinct from God, leading them to posit time as an eternal entity coexisting with Him. This sets the stage for the mutakallimun's counter-argument in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification and philosophers' argument
Start by reading the passage that divides existents. Then follow the philosophers' reasoning step by step.
Notice how the argument moves from a conceptual analysis to a concrete conclusion about time.
2
Identify the two types of existents
Locate the definitions of qadim and hadith. Understand that God alone is qadim; everything else is hadith.
3
Analyze the philosophers' argument structure
Break down the argument: the concept of 'Allah existed in pre-eternity' is either non-existential or existential. The non-existential option is ruled out, so it must be existential. That existential concept is either identical to Allah or other than Him. Identical is impossible, so it is other, and that other is time.
Each step relies on a logical dichotomy and elimination.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why philosophers conclude that time is a real, eternal entity coexisting with God. This is a key point of contention with the mutakallimun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الموجودات على رأى المتكلمين
الموجود إما أن يكون قدما أو حديثا ، أما القديم فهو لا أول لوجوده وهو الله سبحانه وتعالى ، والمحادث ما لوجوده أول وهو ما عداه ..
قالت الفلاسفة : مفهوم قولنا كان الله فى الأزل موجودا إما أن يكون عدميا أو وجوديا والأول باطل وإلا لكان قولنا كان موجودا فى الأزل ثبوتيا فيكون المعدوم موصوفا بالوصف الوجودى وهو محال فثبت أن ذلك المفهوم وجودى ، وهو إما أن يكون عين الله تعالى أو غيره والأول محال ، لأن كونه في الأول غير حاصل الآن وإلا لكان الآن هو الأزل ، وكل ما وجد الآن وجد فى الأزل هذا خلف ، لكن ذاته حاصلة الآن ، فكونه فى الأزل أمر زائد على ذاته وذلك الأمر كان موجودا في الأزل وقد كان في الأزل مع الله تعالى غيره ، ثم ذلك الغير هو الذى يلحقه معنى كان ويكون لذاته وذلك هو الزمان والزمان موجود فى الأزل . قال المتكلمون : معنى كون الله تعالى قديما أنا لو قدرنا أزمنة لا أول لها لكان الله تعالى موجودا معها بأسرها ، وبما يقرر ذلك أنا لو اعتبرنا الزمان في ماهية الحدوث والقدم لكان ذلك الزمان إما أن يكون قديما أو حادثا ؛ فإن كان قديما مع أنه ليس له زمان آخر ، فقد صار القدم معقولا من غير اعتبار الزمان ، وإذا عقل ذلك في موضع فليعقل ذلك فى كل موضع ، وإن كان حادثا لم يعتبر فى حدوثه زمان آخر لاستحالة أن يكون الزمان زمان آخر ، وإذا عقل الحدوث فى نفس الزمان من غير اعتبار زمان فليعقل مثله فى سائر المواضع )
Division of existents according to the mutakallimun:
The existent is either qadim (eternal) or hadith (originated). As for the qadim, it has no beginning to its existence, and it is Allah, glorified and exalted. The hadith is that whose existence has a beginning, and it is whatever is other than Him.
The philosophers said: The concept of our saying 'Allah existed in pre-eternity (azal)' is either non-existent or existent. The first is false; otherwise our saying 'He existed in pre-eternity' would be affirmative, so the non-existent would be described by an existential attribute, which is impossible. Thus it is established that that concept is existential. And it is either identical to Allah or other than Him. The first is impossible, because His being in the past is not realized now; otherwise now would be pre-eternity. And everything that exists now would have existed in pre-eternity, which is a contradiction. But His essence is realized now, so His being in pre-eternity is something additional to His essence, and that thing existed in pre-eternity, and in pre-eternity there was with Allah something other than Him. Then that other is what is subject to the meaning of 'was' and 'will be' in itself, and that is time, and time existed in pre-eternity.
The mutakallimun said: The meaning of Allah being qadim is that if we were to suppose infinite time periods, Allah would exist with all of them. And what establishes that is that if we consider time in the quiddity of origination and eternity, that time would either be qadim or hadith. If it is qadim, while it has no other time, then eternity has become conceivable without considering time. And if it can be conceived in one case, let it be conceived in every case. And if it is hadith, then its origination is not considered to require another time, because it is impossible for time to have another time. So if origination is conceived in time itself without considering time, let the same be conceived in all other cases.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage begins with a classification: existents are either qadim (eternal, having no beginning) or hadith (originated, having a beginning). God alone is qadim; everything else is hadith. The philosophers then present an argument: the concept of 'Allah existed in pre-eternity' must be something existential (wujudi), not non-existential ('adami). If it were non-existential, then the statement 'Allah existed' would be negative, but it is affirmative. So it is existential. It cannot be identical to God because His being in the past is not a current attribute; otherwise now would be pre-eternity, which is false. Hence it must be something other than God, and that other thing is time (zaman), which is eternal and coexistent with God. The mutakallimun respond by defining God's qidam differently: it means that if we suppose infinite time periods, God would exist with all of them. They argue that both eternity (qidam) and origination (huduth) can be understood without positing time as a real entity. If time were considered in the definition, it would itself need to be either qadim or hadith, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, both concepts are intelligible without reference to real time, and God's qidam does not imply a temporal dimension.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
أزل
مفهوم
وجودي
عدمي
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's pre-eternity is a purely negative concept (i.e., just 'no beginning').
Correction: The philosophers argue that the statement 'Allah existed in pre-eternity' is affirmative, so the concept must be something positive, not a mere negation.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can't the concept of 'Allah existed in pre-eternity' be identical to Allah?
A) Because Allah cannot be described by temporal concepts.
B) Because if it were identical, then now would be pre-eternity, which is false.
C) Because the concept is non-existential.
D) Because time is separate from Allah.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the very concept of God's pre-eternity logically entails the existence of time as an eternal entity distinct from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'aḥaduṣ-ṣamad' (Allah, the Eternal, the Absolute), do you genuinely conceive of a God without any temporal beginning, or does your mind unavoidably picture a 'time' before creation? In that moment, are you closer to the philosophers or the mutakallimun?
?
The philosophers argue that saying 'Allah existed in pre-eternity' logically forces time into existence alongside Him. If you reject this, on what grounds do you do so? Does your rejection come from scripture, reason, or both? And does it leave you with a coherent concept of 'pre-eternity' or just a comfortable mystery?
?
If time is a created thing, then even the statement 'Allah was before time' is linguistically problematic. Have you ever felt the weight of this difficulty in your heart? How does it shape your worship or trust in a God whose existence precedes all categories, including time itself?

The Mutakallimun's Response: Eternity Without Time
The mutakallimun's counter-argument that eternity and origination do not require time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that God's eternity and the origination of things can be conceived without positing time as a real entity.

Core Idea
Eternity and origination are intelligible without assuming a real time.

In response to the philosophers' claim that time must be a real eternal entity, the mutakallimun offer a sharp counter-argument. They define God's qidam (eternity) in terms of His coexistence with all possible times, and then show that both eternity and origination can be understood without invoking time at all. This lesson unpacks their argument and its implications for Kalam ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the mutakallimun's definition and argument
Carefully read the passage where the mutakallimun define God's being qadim and then argue against the necessity of time.
Notice they use a conditional and a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the definition of God's qidam
The mutakallimun say: God is qadim means that if we suppose infinite times, He exists with all of them. This avoids positing time as a real entity.
This definition is operational, not ontological.
3
Analyze the argument about time in quiddity
They argue: if time were essential to the definitions of eternity (qidam) and origination (huduth), then time itself would have to be either eternal or originated, leading to a regress or circularity.
This shows that time cannot be a real constituent of these concepts.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since both eternity and origination can be conceived without reference to time (as shown by the cases of time itself), they are intelligible without positing a real time entity.
This undermines the philosophers' argument for the reality of time.

6 min
Eternality and Origination: Defining Qadim and Muhdath
Razi defines qadim and muhdath, presents the philosophers' objection about time, and gives the mutakallimun's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun define qadim and muhdath, the philosophers' objection about time, and the mutakallimun's response that eternity is not contingent upon time.

Core Idea
Eternity is not defined by time; it is the absence of a beginning.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi lays out the foundational distinction between the eternal (qadim) and the originated (muhdath). He then presents a key philosophical objection that seems to force time into eternity, and finally provides the mutakallimun's response, which frees the concept of eternity from any dependence on time.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Begin by reading the opening two sentences where Razi defines qadim and muhdath. Note the simple clarity: qadim has no beginning, muhdath has a beginning.
This is the foundation of all kalam arguments about God.
2
Analyze the philosophers' objection
Read the philosophers' argument carefully. They argue that the concept 'God existed in pre-eternity' implies an existent attribute other than God, and that attribute is time, making time eternal.
Notice the logical steps: the concept is either non-existent or existent; it cannot be non-existent, so it is existent; it cannot be identical to God, so it is other; that other is time.
3
Understand the mutakallimun's response
Now read the mutakallimun's reply. They argue that eternity and originatedness can be conceived without reference to time. They use a thought experiment: if time is eternal, eternity is still conceivable; if time is originated, its own originatedness does not require another time.
The key move: they decouple the concept of 'beginning' from 'time'.
4
Examine the final clarification
Read the last sentence about effect and persistence. Razi clarifies that by 'effect' they do not mean a new thing, but the continuation of an effect, which itself is new in a sense.
This reinforces that qadim does not imply temporal extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الموجودات على رأى المتكلمين
الموجود إما أن يكون قدما أو حديثا، أما القديم فهو لا أول لوجوده وهو الله سبحانه وتعالى، والمحادث ما لوجوده أول وهو ما عداه.
قالت الفلاسفة: مفهوم قولنا كان الله في الأزل موجودا إما أن يكون عدميا أو وجوديا، والأول باطل وإلا لكان قولنا كان موجودا في الأزل ثبوتيا فيكون المعدوم موصوفا بالوصف الوجودي وهو محال، فثبت أن ذلك المفهوم وجودي، وهو إما أن يكون عين الله تعالى أو غيره والأول محال، لأن كونه في الأول غير حاصل الآن وإلا لكان الآن هو الأزل، وكل ما وجد الآن وجد في الأزل هذا خلف، لكن ذاته حاصلة الآن، فكوله في الأزل أمر رائد على ذاته وذلك الأمر كان موجودا في الأزل وقد كان في الأزل مع الله تعالى غيره، ثم ذلك الغير هو الذي يلحقه معنى كان ويكون لذاته وذلك هو الزمان والزمان موجود في الأزل.
قال المتكلمون: معنى كون الله تعالى القديم أنا لو قدرنا أزمنة لا أول لها لكان الله تعالى موجودا معها بأسرها، وبما يقرر ذلك أنا لو اعتبرنا الزمان في ماهية الحدوث والقدم فإن ذلك الزمان إما أن يكون قديما أو حادثا؛ فإن كان قديما مع أنه ليس له زمان آخر، فقد صار القدم معقولا من غير اعتبار الزمان، وإذا عقل ذلك في موضع فليعقل ذلك في كل موضع، وإن كان حادثا لم يعتبر في حدوثه زمان آخر لاستحالة أن يكون الزمان زمان آخر، وإذا عقل الحدوث في نفس الزمان من غير اعتبار زمان فليعقل مثله في سائر المواضع.
وقوله في الجواب: لا نعني بالتأثير تحصيل أمر جديد بل بقاء الأثر لبقاء المؤثر ليس بجديد لأن البقاء المستفاد من المؤثر أمر جديد لولاه لكان الأثر مما لا يبقى.
Division of existents according to the mutakallimun: The existent is either eternal or originated. As for the eternal, it has no beginning to its existence, and it is Allah, glorified and exalted. The originated is that which has a beginning to its existence, and it is other than Him. The philosophers said: The concept of our saying 'God existed in pre-eternity' is either non-existent or existent. The first is invalid, otherwise our saying 'He existed in pre-eternity' would be affirmative, and thus the non-existent would be described by an existential attribute, which is impossible. So it is established that that concept is existent. It is either identical to Allah or other than Him. The first is impossible, because His being in the first [pre-eternity] is not obtained now, otherwise now would be pre-eternity, and everything that exists now would have existed in pre-eternity, which is absurd. But His essence is obtained now, so His being in pre-eternity is an additional [attribute] over His essence, and that attribute existed in pre-eternity, and in pre-eternity there was with Allah something other than Him. Then that other is what is attached to the meaning of 'was' and 'will be' to its essence, and that is time, and time exists in pre-eternity. The mutakallimun said: The meaning of Allah being eternal is that if we were to suppose times with no beginning, Allah would exist with them entirely. And what establishes that is that if we consider time in the essence of originatedness and eternity, then that time is either eternal or originated. If it is eternal, despite its having no other time, then eternity becomes conceivable without consideration of time. And if it is conceivable in one place, let it be conceivable in every place. And if it is originated, another time is not considered in its originatedness due to the impossibility of time having another time. And if originatedness is conceivable in the very essence of time without consideration of time, let the like be conceivable in other places. And his statement in the response: We do not mean by effecting the production of a new thing, but the continuation of the effect due to the continuation of the effector is not new, because the continuation derived from the effector is a new thing; otherwise the effect would be among what does not endure.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. It begins by classifying existents into qadim (eternal) and muhdath (originated). The eternal is defined as that which has no beginning of existence, and the originated as that which has a beginning. Next, Razi presents the philosophers' objection: the concept 'God existed in pre-eternity' must be something existent, not non-existent; if it were non-existent, then affirming it would be attributing an existential quality to a non-existent, which is impossible. Therefore, it is existent. It cannot be identical to God because then His existence in pre-eternity would be the same as His existence now, implying now is pre-eternity. Hence, it is additional to God, and that additional thing is time. Thus, time is eternal. The mutakallimun respond by explaining that eternity does not require time. They argue: if we suppose an infinite series of times, God would exist with them all. To establish this, they consider time in the essence of originatedness and eternity. If time itself is eternal, then eternity is conceived without time; if time is originated, its originatedness is not contingent on another time. Thus, eternity and originatedness can be conceived without time in all cases. Finally, Razi adds a clarification: by 'effect' they do not mean producing a new thing, but the persistence of the effect due to the persistence of the effector, and that persistence is itself new; otherwise the effect would not endure. This shows that the mutakallimun's definition of qadim does not rely on time.

عدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the mutakallimun accept time as eternal, as suggested by the philosophers' objection.
Correction: The mutakallimun do not accept time as eternal. They argue that eternity is not defined by time; it is the absence of a beginning, which can be conceived independently of time.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, what is the meaning of God being qadim (eternal)?
A) That He has no beginning and is not associated with time.
B) That He existed before time and time is eternal.
C) That He is the cause of time and time is originated.
D) That He exists with an infinite series of times.

Key Takeaway
Eternity (qadim) is defined by the absence of a beginning, not by association with time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's eternity means He has no beginning and is completely independent of time, how does that challenge your daily habit of measuring your life by minutes, hours, and years? Do you truly live in awareness of a Lord for whom time is entirely irrelevant?
?
The philosophers argued that talking about God 'before' time logically implies time is eternal. Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as existing 'in' time, perhaps thinking of Him as 'waiting' or 'delaying'? How does the mutakallimun's response free you from such human-like limitations?
?
Knowing that nothing—not even time—is co-eternal with Allah, what does that demand from you in terms of your reliance on Him for every moment? Are there any 'eternal' idols in your life—habits or securities that you treat as if they are timeless? Will you today consciously remind yourself that only Allah is qadim, and all else is muhdath?

The Verbal Dispute on the Eternal and the Agent
Razi presents a verbal dispute and resolves it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi considers the disagreement between the mutakallimun and philosophers over attributing the eternal to an agent to be merely verbal.

Core Idea
Both sides agree that the eternal can only proceed from a necessitating essence, not a free agent.

Razi presents a seemingly sharp disagreement: the mutakallimun say attributing the eternal to an agent is impossible, while the philosophers say it is possible. However, he argues that the disagreement is verbal because both groups ultimately agree on the substantive point. This passage will help you see how theological disputes can often be resolved by clarifying terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
Note what the mutakallimun and philosophers agree on respectively: one says impossible, the other says not impossible temporally.
3
Understand Razi's verbal resolution
He says the mutakallimun do not deny attributing to a necessitating essence (al-mu'aththir al-mujib bi al-dhat), only to a free agent. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
This is the key insight.
4
Connect to the examples
The examples of Abu Hashim and Abu al-Husayn show that mutakallimun effectively treat states as eternal, even if they avoid the term.
5
Grasp the final conclusion
Razi concludes that all agree on attributing the eternal to an eternal necessitator, but not to a free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خواص القديم والمحدث
( ) مسألة : اتفق المتكلمون على أن القديم يستحيل إسناده إلى الفاعل واتفقت الفلاسفة على أنه غير ممتنع زمانا )
فإن العالم قديم عندهم زماناً مع أنه فعل الله تعالى ، وعندى أن الخلاف في هذا المقام لفظن ، لأن المتكلمين لم ينموا إسناد القديم إلى المؤثر الموجب بالذات ، ولذلك زعم مثبت و الحال منا أن عالمية الله تعالى وعليه أن العالمية مطلة بالعلم ، وزعم أبو هاشم أن العالمية والقادرية والحبيبة والموجودية معللة بحالة قديمان ، مع خامسة مع أن الكل قديم، وزعم أبو الحسين أن العالمية حالة معللة بالذات، وهؤلاء وإن كانوا يمتنعون عن إطلاق لفظ القديم على هذه الأحوال لكنهم يعطون المعنى في الحقيقة 
وأما الفلاسفة فإنهم إنما جوزوا إسناد العالم إلى البارى تعالى ، لكنه عندهم موجب بالذات حتى لو اعتقدوا فيه كونه فاعلا بالاختيار الماجوز واكونه موجد للعالم القديم، فظهر من هذا اتفاق الكل على جواز إسناد القديم إلى الموجب القديم وامتناع إسناده إلى المختار
(1) أقول إنما ذهب المتكلمون إلى أن القديم يستحيل إسناده إلى الفاعل لا لقولهم علة الحاجة هو الحدوث، فإن هذا القول مختص ببعضهم كما مر لكن لقوطم بأن مأسوى الله تعالى وصفاته محدث وللأحوال التي ذكرها عند مثبتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة فلا يوصف بالقدم على ما ذكره ، وفى تفسير القديم بما لا أول لوجوده إلا أن تغير التفسير وتقول القديم مالا أول لثبوته ، على أن الوجود والثبوت عنده مترادفان لكنا نقول ههنا ما قال المتكلمون وليس عند بعضهم معناهما واحداً وأبو الحسين لا يقول بالحال لكنه يقول العلم صفة الله قديمة معلقة بالذات وأما أصحاب أبي الحسن الأشعرى فيقولون بصفات قديمة لكنهم يقولون لا هى الذات ولا غيرها ، فلذلك لا يطلبون المعلولية عليها ، أو الحق في أن جميعهم أعطوا معنى القديم في الحقيقة على هذه الصفات معه فإن إبائهم عن إطلاق لفظ القديم عليها ليس بحقيق .
Properties of the eternal and the originated. ( ) Issue: The mutakallimun agreed that it is impossible to attribute the eternal to the agent, and the philosophers agreed that it is not impossible temporally. For the world is eternal temporally according to them while it is the act of Allah. And in my view, the disagreement in this place is verbal, because the mutakallimun did not deny attributing the eternal to the effectual cause that necessitates by essence. Therefore, the establisher of states claimed that Allah's being omniscient and His knowledge [?] that omniscience is necessitated by knowledge, and Abu Hashim claimed that omniscience, omnipotence, life, and existence are necessitated by an eternal state, with a fifth [?], even though all are eternal. And Abu al-Husayn claimed that omniscience is a state necessitated by the essence. And these, even though they refrain from applying the term 'eternal' to these states, they give the meaning in reality. As for the philosophers, they allowed attributing the world to the Creator, but according to them He is a necessitator by essence, even if they believed He is a free agent [?], and He is the originator of the eternal world. So it became clear from this that all agree on the permissibility of attributing the eternal to the eternal necessitator and the impossibility of attributing it to the free agent. (1) I say: The mutakallimun held that it is impossible to attribute the eternal to the agent not because of their saying that the cause of need is originatedness, for this saying is specific to some of them as has passed, but because they say that everything other than Allah and His attributes is originated, and the states that the establishers mention are neither existent nor non-existent, so they are not described as eternal according to what he mentioned. And in the interpretation of the eternal as that which has no beginning of existence, unless one changes the interpretation and says the eternal is that which has no beginning of affirmation, given that existence and affirmation are synonymous according to him, but we say here what the mutakallimun said. And according to some, their meanings are not one, and Abu al-Husayn does not affirm the state but says knowledge is an eternal attribute of Allah attached to the essence. As for the followers of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, they affirm eternal attributes but say they are neither the essence nor other than it, therefore they do not seek effect upon them. Or the truth is that all of them gave the meaning of eternal in reality to these attributes, for their refusal to apply the term 'eternal' to them is not real.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a juridical-style discussion (مسألة) concerning the properties of the eternal and originated. Razi states that the mutakallimun agree it is impossible to attribute the eternal (as effect) to an agent, while the philosophers allow it because they consider the world eternal yet caused by God. Razi comments that the disagreement is merely verbal because the mutakallimun do not deny attributing the eternal to a necessitating essence (المؤثر الموجب بالذات), only to a free agent. He then illustrates with examples: the mutakallimun who affirm states (أحوال) like Abū Hāshim and Abū al-Ḥusayn apply the concept of eternal to these states without using the term. The philosophers, on the other hand, attribute the eternal world to God as a necessitator (موجب بالذات), not a free agent. Thus, the core agreement is that the eternal can proceed from an eternal necessitator but not from a free agent. The footnote comment clarifies that the mutakallimun's position is based on everything other than God and His attributes being originated, and that states are not described as qadim because they are neither existent nor non-existent. It also discusses different interpretations of qadim and the views of Abū al-Ḥusayn and Ash'aris.

فاعل
موجب بالذات
مختار
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the mutakallimun and philosophers fundamentally disagree on whether the eternal can be caused.
Correction: Razi shows they agree that the eternal can proceed from a necessitating essence; the disagreement is only about the type of agent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the disagreement between the mutakallimun and philosophers over attributing the eternal to an agent considered verbal?
A) Because both sides agree that God is a free agent.
B) Because the mutakallimun allow attributing the eternal to a necessitating essence, and the philosophers only allow it from a necessitating essence, not a free agent.
C) Because the philosophers deny that God is an agent at all.
D) Because the mutakallimun use the term 'agent' differently.

Key Takeaway
The disagreement is verbal because both sides agree that the eternal can only arise from a necessitating cause, not a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about why God created the universe, do you assume He freely chose to create, or do you think His essence necessitated creation? How does Razi’s conclusion—that both sides agree only a necessitating cause can produce an eternal effect—make you uncomfortable about your own assumption? Will you examine your belief more deeply today?
?
In your own arguments about religion or theology, how often do you realize that the disagreement might be verbal rather than substantive? Is there a specific dispute you are currently involved in where you could look for underlying agreement? What would it cost you to admit that the difference is merely words?
?
Razi uses the example of divine attributes to show that people sometimes avoid certain labels (like 'eternal') while still holding the concept. Are there Islamic beliefs you hold but hesitate to name openly? What would change if you started affirming them clearly, even if it challenges your comfort zone?

The Mutakallimun's Reasoning and the Status of States
Razi explains the reasoning behind the mutakallimun's position and nuances of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's true reasons for denying that the eternal can be attributed to a free agent, and how they treat states and attributes differently.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun deny attributing eternal to a free agent because everything other than God is originated, and states are neither existent nor non-existent.

In this footnote, Razi delves deeper into the mutakallimun's position, correcting a possible misunderstanding. He clarifies that their denial is not based on the principle that 'the cause of need is originatedness', but rather on the conviction that everything besides God and His attributes is originated. He also discusses the metaphysical status of states and how different schools handle the eternity of divine attributes, showing that even when they avoid the term 'eternal', they still affirm the meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote carefully
This passage is dense; read it slowly, paying attention to the different positions mentioned.
2
Identify the mutakallimun's real reason
Razi says it is not because of the saying 'the cause of need is originatedness', but because everything besides God and His attributes is originated, and states are neither existent nor non-existent.
This is a correction of a common assumption.
3
Understand the status of states
States, according to their proponents, are neither existent nor non-existent, so they cannot be described as 'eternal' in the usual sense.
4
Compare the views of different theologians
Note how Abu al-Husayn (who does not affirm states) and the Ash'aris treat attributes differently from the earlier mutakallimun.
Abu al-Husayn says knowledge is an eternal attribute attached to the essence; Ash'aris say attributes are neither the essence nor other than it.
5
Grasp the final point
Razi concludes that all of them effectively give the meaning of 'eternal' to these attributes, even if they avoid the term.

Source Text
