According to Razi, what is the main reason the mutakallimun deny attributing the eternal to a free agent?
A) Because they say the cause of need is originatedness.
B) Because they say everything other than Allah and His attributes is originated, and states are neither existent nor non-existent.
C) Because they deny the existence of states.
D) Because they believe only God can be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's denial is rooted in the originatedness of all things except God and His attributes, and the special metaphysical status of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that scholars who refuse the term 'eternal' for God's attributes still affirm the meaning. Do you cling to theological labels without grasping the underlying truth? Ask Allah to cleanse your heart from attachment to mere terminology.
?
The mutakallimun were meticulous to avoid anything implying originatedness in God. In your own thoughts about Allah, do you ever unconsciously attribute change or deficiency? What one practice can you adopt to elevate your conception of Allah?
?
Razi’s clarification demands intellectual humility. Are you willing to admit that your understanding of Allah's attributes may be shallow? Commit to studying one attribute deeply this week, seeking to know Allah as He deserves.

Non-Existence Does Not Apply to the Eternal
Razi declares that non-existence is not applicable to the eternal and flags it as a premise for the proof of origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal is a key premise in the kalam proof of origination.

Core Idea
The eternal cannot cease to exist – this is the foundation of the proof of the world's origin.

Imam Razi presents a foundational principle in Islamic theology: non-existence cannot be attributed to the eternal (al-qadim). This principle may seem simple, but it is one of the essential premises for proving that the world is originated (hadith al-‘alam). In this lesson, we will explore this statement and its logical role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through.
2
Understand the doctrinal claim
What does it mean that non-existence does not apply to the eternal? Reflect on why the eternal cannot be subject to non-existence.
This directly connects to the nature of the eternal being immutable.
3
Connect to the proof of origination
Razi mentions this claim is a premise for the proof of origination. Why would this premise be necessary? Consider the logic: if the eternal could cease to exist, then the world might not need a creator. But if the eternal cannot cease, then the world must have originated.
This step links the lesson to broader kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: العدم لا يصح على القديم . ولما كانت هذه المسألة إحدى مقدمات مسئلة الحدوث رأينا أن نذكر برهانها هناك .
A question: Non-existence does not apply to the eternal. And since this question is one of the premises of the question of origination (hadith), we considered that we would mention its proof there.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This short passage is a brief doctrinal statement (a 'mas'ala') that non-existence (al-'adam) is not applicable to the eternal (al-qadim). Razi notes that this proposition is one of the premises needed for the famous proof of the origination of the world (hadith al-‘alam), but he chooses not to elaborate the proof here, deferring it to the discussion of origination itself. The passage functions as a placeholder, alerting the reader to the logical structure: to prove that something is originated, one must first establish that the eternal does not cease to exist. Thus, the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal is a key premise in the kalam argument for creation. By stating it here, Razi signals the interconnectedness of the arguments, even if he does not fully develop it in this section.

Key Terms
العدم
القديم
الحدوث
المقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-existence could be attributed to the eternal (God) in any sense, such as before creation or after annihilation.
Correction: Razi states plainly that non-existence does not apply to the eternal; the eternal is immutable and cannot cease to exist.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi mention that non-existence does not apply to the eternal?
A) To define the eternal.
B) Because it is a necessary premise for the proof of origination (hadith).
C) To refute the Harranians.
D) To prove the existence of God.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence cannot be attributed to the eternal, and this principle is a foundational premise for proving the world's origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The eternal cannot cease – your own existence is fleeting. Does your attachment to temporary things reflect a deeper denial of that reality? What is one worldly thing you need to release because it distracts you from the Eternal?
?
Razi says this premise is foundational for proving the world's origin. Have you internalized the fact that your accountability before the Eternal is permanent? What would change in your daily priorities if you lived as if God's existence was as certain as your own? Commit to one action today that acknowledges His permanence.

The Philosophers' Proof for Eternal Time
Philosophers' proof for an eternal time based on priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that time must be eternal based on the notion of priority (al-qabliyya) and why they considered it an existential attribute.

Core Idea
Priority is not non-existence; it requires an eternal time.

The philosophers claim that every originated thing has non-existence before its existence. This 'beforeness' (priority) cannot be the same as non-existence, because non-existence is identical before and after. Instead, it is an attribute that must belong to an existing subject—something that persists and provides a measure of priority. That subject, they argue, is time itself, which must therefore be eternal, without beginning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of priority
The passage begins by distinguishing priority from non-existence. Note that non-existence before and after are identical, so priority must be something else.
2
Trace the argument for an existing subject
Priority is an attribute that requires a subject. Since every originated thing has priority before it, there must be an eternal chain of priorities, and the ultimate subject is time.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Time, as the subject of priority, has no beginning. This challenges the Islamic creed that the universe is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدة فقالوا : كل محدث فعدمه قبل وجوده ، فتلك القبلية ليست نفس العدم، فإن العدم قبل كالعدم بعد وليس القبل بعد، وهي صفة وجودية تستدعي موصوفاً موجوداً فقبل ذلك الحادث شيء موصوف بالقبلية لا إلى أول، فهذا قبليات لا أول لها، والذي يلحقه القبلية لذاته هو الزمان، فهنا زمان لا أول له . والجواب : أن تقدم عدم الحادث على وجوده لو وجب أن يكون بالزمان لكان تقدم كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان على وجوده بالزمان، ولكان تقدم البارى تعالى على هذا الجزء من الزمان، فيلزم أن يكون الله تعالى زمانيا وأن يكون الزمان زمانيا فهما محالان .

أقول: إنهم يقولون القبلية والبعدية يلحقان الزمان لذاته ولغير الزمان بسبب الزمان، والوجود والعدم لما لم يدخل الزمان في مفهومهما احتاجا في صيرورتهما بعد وقبل إلى زمان، أما أجزاء الزمان فلا تحتاج إلى غير أنفسها ولا العدم بالقياس إليها في كونها بعد أو قبل إلى غيرها، وأما البارى تعالى وكل ما هو علة الزمان او شرط وجوده فلا يكون في الزمان ولا معه إلا فى التوهم، حيث يقبها الوهم على الزمانيات فهذا ما قالوه ههنا.
As for duration, they said: Every originated thing has its non-existence before its existence. That priority is not the same as non-existence, for non-existence before is like non-existence after, and 'before' is not 'after'. It is an existential attribute requiring an existent subject. So before that originated thing there is something described with priority without a first. So there are priorities without a beginning. That which priority attaches to by itself is time. So there is a time without a beginning. The answer: That the precedence of the non-existence of the originated thing to its existence, if it were necessarily by time, then the precedence of each part of time to its existence would be by time, and the precedence of the Exalted Creator to this part of time would be by time, resulting in God being temporal and time being temporal, both impossible.

I say: They say that priority and posteriority attach to time by itself and to other than time by time. Existence and non-existence, since time is not included in their concept, they need time in becoming after and before. As for the parts of time, they do not need other than themselves, nor does non-existence relative to them in being after or before need other. As for the Exalted Creator and everything that is the cause of time or condition of its existence, it is not in time nor with it except in imagination, where the imagination [؟accepts?] them upon temporal things. This is what they said here.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers regarding duration (al-mudda) and the refutation. The passage begins with the philosophers' argument: every originated thing has non-existence before its existence. That 'priority' (al-qabliyya) is not identical to non-existence, because non-existence before is like non-existence after, and 'before' is not 'after'. Thus priority is an existential attribute requiring an existent subject. So there must be something described with priority before the originated thing, and this chain has no first member. That which priority attaches to by itself is time, so there is time without a beginning. The refutation follows: if the precedence of non-existence to existence were necessarily by time, then every part of time would itself be preceded by time (an infinite regress), and the Creator would be temporal, leading to absurdities. The marginal note (أقول) clarifies the philosophers' position: priority and posteriority attach to time by itself and to other things through time; existence and non-existence require time for temporal ordering, but the parts of time do not need an external time. The Creator, being the cause of time, is not in time except in imagination.

Key Terms
المدة
القبلية
الزمان
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that priority is just a mental concept with no real existence.
Correction: The philosophers argue that priority is an existential attribute, meaning it exists in reality and requires a real subject.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' argument, why must priority be an existential attribute?
A) Because non-existence changes over time.
B) Because it is identical to non-existence.
C) Because non-existence before and after are the same, so priority must be something else.
D) Because time is a mental construct.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that priority is an attribute requiring an eternal time, making time uncreated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the philosophers argue for an eternal time based on the attribute of priority, do you feel a tension between your theological belief that Allah created time and this rational argument? How do you personally resolve that tension? What single verse or hadith comes to mind that anchors your conviction?
?
The philosophers claim that priority is an existential attribute requiring a real subject. But is it possible that you sometimes treat abstract concepts as if they are real entities? How might that subtle misstep affect your understanding of Allah’s attributes and actions?
?
If this argument were presented to you by someone seeking to undermine your faith, would you be able to identify its flaw from the Quran and Sunnah? What is the most immediate Islamic response you could give, and why is it important that you know it?

Razi's Refutation and the Transcendence of Allah
Razi's refutation and clarification of the philosophers' position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's refutation of the philosophers' proof for eternal time and how he preserves Allah's transcendence over time.

Core Idea
If time were eternal, the Creator would be temporal—an impossibility.

Razi responds to the philosophers' argument by showing that it leads to absurd consequences: if the priority of non-existence to existence were always mediated by time, then each part of time would itself require a prior time, leading to an infinite regress. Moreover, the Creator would then be subject to time, contradicting His transcendence. The marginal note clarifies that the philosophers themselves admit that the Creator is not in time except in imagination.

Guided Path
1
Understand the refutation's logic
Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the priority of non-existence to existence is always by time, then the priority of each part of time to its own existence would also require time, leading to an infinite regress.
2
Apply to the Creator
The Creator's priority to time would then also be by time, making Him temporal—an impossibility for the Islamic conception of God.
3
Read the marginal clarification
The marginal note (أقول) explains that the philosophers actually hold that priority and posteriority attach to time by itself, and to other things through time. The Creator, being the cause of time, is not in time except in imagination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدة فقالوا : كل محدث فعدمه قبل وجوده ، فتلك القبلية ليست نفس العدم، فإن العدم قبل كالعدم بعد وليس القبل بعد، وهي صفة وجودية تستدعي موصوفاً موجوداً فقبل ذلك الحادث شيء موصوف بالقبلية لا إلى أول، فهذا قبليات لا أول لها، والذي يلحقه القبلية لذاته هو الزمان، فهنا زمان لا أول له . والجواب : أن تقدم عدم الحادث على وجوده لو وجب أن يكون بالزمان لكان تقدم كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان على وجوده بالزمان، ولكان تقدم البارى تعالى على هذا الجزء من الزمان، فيلزم أن يكون الله تعالى زمانيا وأن يكون الزمان زمانيا فهما محالان .

أقول: إنهم يقولون القبلية والبعدية يلحقان الزمان لذاته ولغير الزمان بسبب الزمان، والوجود والعدم لما لم يدخل الزمان في مفهومهما احتاجا في صيرورتهما بعد وقبل إلى زمان، أما أجزاء الزمان فلا تحتاج إلى غير أنفسها ولا العدم بالقياس إليها في كونها بعد أو قبل إلى غيرها، وأما البارى تعالى وكل ما هو علة الزمان او شرط وجوده فلا يكون في الزمان ولا معه إلا فى التوهم، حيث يقبها الوهم على الزمانيات فهذا ما قالوه ههنا.
As for duration, they said: Every originated thing has its non-existence before its existence. That priority is not the same as non-existence, for non-existence before is like non-existence after, and 'before' is not 'after'. It is an existential attribute requiring an existent subject. So before that originated thing there is something described with priority without a first. So there are priorities without a beginning. That which priority attaches to by itself is time. So there is a time without a beginning. The answer: That the precedence of the non-existence of the originated thing to its existence, if it were necessarily by time, then the precedence of each part of time to its existence would be by time, and the precedence of the Exalted Creator to this part of time would be by time, resulting in God being temporal and time being temporal, both impossible.

I say: They say that priority and posteriority attach to time by itself and to other than time by time. Existence and non-existence, since time is not included in their concept, they need time in becoming after and before. As for the parts of time, they do not need other than themselves, nor does non-existence relative to them in being after or before need other. As for the Exalted Creator and everything that is the cause of time or condition of its existence, it is not in time nor with it except in imagination, where the imagination [؟accepts?] them upon temporal things. This is what they said here.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers regarding duration (al-mudda) and the refutation. The passage begins with the philosophers' argument: every originated thing has non-existence before its existence. That 'priority' (al-qabliyya) is not identical to non-existence, because non-existence before is like non-existence after, and 'before' is not 'after'. Thus priority is an existential attribute requiring an existent subject. So there must be something described with priority before the originated thing, and this chain has no first member. ?

Imam Razi's Refutation of Time's Necessity
Imam Razi refutes a proof that time would be necessarily existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the argument that time would be necessarily existent by distinguishing between contradictory and possible suppositions of non-existence.

Core Idea
Time's non-existence seems impossible only when we mix it with its own existence.

Imam Razi continues his critique of proofs for a necessary existent by examining an argument based on time. The argument claims that if time existed, it would have to be necessary, but Razi shows this reasoning fails due to a subtle conflation. Understanding this refutation sharpens our ability to evaluate modal arguments about existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full English translation. Notice the structure: a conditional argument followed by a refutation and identification of the error.
The OCR has been corrected, but be aware of potential minor errors.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Find the statement 'if time were existent, it would be necessarily existent' and trace how the arguer supports it using the concept of posteriority.
The arguer supposes time's non-existence after existence leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand Razi's response
Notice how Razi distinguishes between two ways of supposing time's non-existence: one that presupposes its existence (contradictory) and one that does not (possible).
Razi's key move is to reject the entailment by showing the assumption is already loaded.
4
Reflect on the source of error
Razi identifies two sources: analogizing time to things within time, and coupling existence with non-existence. Consider how these errors undermine the argument.
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها: الزمان لو كان موجودا لكان واجب الوجود لذاته وفساد التالي يدل على فساد المقدم، وبيان الشرطية أنه لو كان موجودا وفرضناه قابلا للعدم فليفرض أنه عدم فيكون عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا توجد مع القبل، وهذه البعدية لا تتحقق إلا عند تحقق الزمان فإذا يلزم من فرض عدم الزمان وجوده وذلك محال. فإذا لمجرد فرض عدمه يستلزم المحال، فإذا كان فرض عدمه محال فهو واجب لذاته. وإنما قلنا أنه يستحيل أن يكون واجبا لذاته لان كل جزء منه حادث وممكن والمجموع ينقوم بالأجزاء والمنقوم بالممكن المحدث يستحيل أن يكون واجبا لذاته. أقول: فرض الزمان بعد وجوده يكون فرض عدمه مع وجوده ويلزم منه المحال لاشتماله على عدم الشيء ووجوده، وفرض عدم الزمان وحده ممكن إذا لم يقترن ذلك العدم بقبل أو بعد، وهذا الغلط نشأ من قياس الزمان على ما في الزمان ومن اقتران وجود الشيء بعدمه.
The third: if time were existent, it would be necessarily existent (wājib al-wujūd) by itself, and the corruption of the consequent indicates the corruption of the antecedent. The demonstration of the conditional: if it were existent and we suppose it possible for it to undergo non-existence, then let it be supposed that it becomes non-existent. Its non-existence after its existence would be a posteriority that does not exist with the prior, and this posteriority is only realized when time is realized; therefore from the supposition of time's non-existence its existence follows, and that is impossible. So merely supposing its non-existence necessitates an impossibility; thus if its supposition of non-existence is impossible, it is necessarily existent by itself. We say that it is impossible for it to be necessarily existent because every part of it is temporal and possible, and the whole is constituted by the parts, and what is constituted by possible temporal things cannot be necessarily existent by itself. I say: The supposition of time after its existence is a supposition of its non-existence along with its existence, and that necessitates an impossibility because it involves both the non-existence and existence of a thing. But the supposition of time's non-existence alone is possible if that non-existence is not coupled with a 'before' or 'after'. This error arises from analogizing time to what is in time, and from coupling the existence of a thing with its non-existence.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a proof that time must be necessarily existent. The argument claims that if time were possible, assuming its non-existence would entail its existence (since posteriority requires time), thus it is necessary. The response distinguishes between supposing time's non-existence after its existence (which is contradictory) and supposing it alone without reference to prior/posterior, which is possible. Hence time is possible, not necessary.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd): necessarily existent
ممكن (mumkin): possible
العدم (al-ʿadam): non-existence
البعدية (al-baʿdiyya): posteriority

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any supposition of time's non-existence leads to a contradiction, proving time's necessity.
Correction: Only the supposition that couples non-existence with existence (i.e., non-existence after existence) yields a contradiction. The simple supposition of non-existence without reference to before/after is possible.

Checkpoint
What is the source of the error in the third argument according to Imam Razi?
A) Assuming time is composed of parts
B) Analogizing time to what is in time and coupling existence with non-existence
C) Denying that time has parts
D) Assuming time is a substance

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi shows that the argument for time's necessity fails because it mistakenly assumes that any supposition of non-existence must be accompanied by temporal relations, conflating a conditional impossibility with an absolute one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you ever assume that time is an absolute, necessary backdrop? How might this assumption affect your understanding of Allah as the only truly necessary Being?
?
Have you ever confused a conditioned impossibility (like time's non-existence after existence) with an absolute impossibility, leading you to accept a flawed argument in your faith or life? What will you do to guard your heart from such logical errors?
?
The error in this passage is analogizing time to things within time. Are there areas where you unknowingly analogize Allah to His creation? Pause and name one such tendency. Commit now to correcting it using the knowledge from this lesson.

Second Argument Against the Existence of Time
Imam Razi presents the second argument that time is nonexistent and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the existence of time and Imam Razi's refutation clarifying that the 'now' is a boundary, not a part of time.

Core Idea
The now is a common boundary, not a part of time.

This lesson presents the second argument that time cannot exist because past and future are nonexistent and the present moment leads to contradictions. Imam Razi then refutes this by explaining that the now is not a part of time but a boundary, analogous to a point on a line. Understanding this distinction resolves the apparent difficulties.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the initial presentation of the argument (first part of the Arabic passage) and its translation. Note how the argument tries to show time's nonexistence by analyzing the present moment.
The argument assumes the now is a part of time.
2
Identify the dilemma
The argument claims the now must be either divisible or indivisible, both leading to contradictions. Identify these two branches and their consequences.
3
Read the refutation
Then read the marginal note (أقول) which is the refutation. Notice how it redefines the now as a common boundary.
4
Compare the two views
Contrast the assumption in the argument (that the now is a part of time) with the refutation's position (that it is a boundary). See how this dissolves the contradictions.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider the analogy of a point on a line and the concept of 'nonexistence in a specific domain' vs absolute nonexistence.
This is the core insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: أن الزمان إما الماضى أو المستقبل أو الحال ، ولا شك أن الماضى والمستقبل معدومان ، أما الحال فهو الآن ، وهو إما أن يتكون منقسما أو لا يكون ، فإن كان منقسما لم توجد أجزاؤه مما فلا يكون الذي فرضناه موجودا موجودا هذا خلف . وإن لم يكن منقسما كان عدمه دفعة لا محالة وعند فنائه يحدث أمر آخر دفعة فيلزم منه تعالى الآنات، ويلزم منه تركب الجسم من نقط متتابعة هذا خلف (1)
أقول : الزمان أما الماضى وإما المستقبل وليس له قسم هو الآن إنما الآن فصل مشترك بين الماضى والمستقبل كالنقطة فى الخط، والماضى الصرف ليس بمعدوم مطلقا إنما هو معدوم فى المستقبل والمستقبل معدوم في الماضي وكلاهما في الآن وكل واحد منهما موجود في حده ، وليس عدم شيء في شيء هو عدمه مطلقا، فإن السماء معدوم فى البيت وليس المعدوم فى موضعه ، ولو كان الآن جزءا من الزمان لما أمكن قسمة الزمان إلى قسمين من الغداة إلى الآن ومن الآن إلى العشاء، فإن كان الآن جزءا لم تكن القسمة صحيحة ولا أمكن قسمة مقدار من الزمان إلى قسمين، فالآن موجود وهو عرض حال فى الزمان كالفصل المشترك في الخط وليس بجزء من الزمان وفنائه إلا تغير زمان فلا يلزم منه تنافى الآنات.
And the second [argument]: Time is either past, future, or present. There is no doubt that the past and future are nonexistent. As for the present, it is the now. It is either divisible or not. If it is divisible, its parts do not exist simultaneously, so what we assumed to exist does not exist—this is a contradiction. If it is indivisible, it necessarily ceases instantly, and at its annihilation another thing occurs instantly, leading to a succession of nows, and leading to the composition of a body from consecutive points—this is a contradiction. (1) I say: Time is either past or future, and it has no part that is the now. Rather, the now is a common boundary between past and future, like a point on a line. The pure past is not absolutely nonexistent; rather, it is nonexistent in the future, and the future is nonexistent in the past. Both are in the now, and each is existent in its own limit. The nonexistence of a thing in something is not its absolute nonexistence, for the sky is nonexistent in a house, but a nonexistent thing is not in its place. If the now were a part of time, it would not be possible to divide time into two parts: from morning to now and from now to evening. If the now were a part, the division would not be correct, nor would it be possible to divide a quantity of time into two parts. Thus the now is existent, and it is an accidental state in time, like the common point on a line, not a part of time. Its annihilation is merely a change of time, so a succession of nows does not necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents and then refutes the second argument against the reality of time. The argument assumes time is either past, future, or present. Past and future are clearly nonexistent, leaving only the present moment. The present moment (now) is then analyzed for divisibility. If divisible, its parts cannot coexist, so the now cannot be existent. If indivisible, it must vanish instantly, and another now appears, leading to an infinite succession of indivisible nows, which would compose a body from consecutive points—an impossibility. The marginal note (أقول) responds by clarifying that the now is not a part of time but a common boundary (فصل مشترك) between past and future, like a point on a line. The past and future are not absolutely nonexistent; each exists in its own domain. The now as a boundary is existent as an accident (عرض حال) in time. Thus, the argument's conclusion that the now must be either divisible or leads to infinite nows is invalid because the now is not a part of time.

Key Terms
الزمان
الآن
الفصل المشترك
العرض
الماضي
المستقبل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The present moment (now) is a part of time that can be divided like any other part.
Correction: The now is actually a common boundary between past and future, like a point on a line; it is not a part of time itself. This resolves the apparent contradictions about its divisibility.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal note, what is the nature of the 'now' (الآن)?
A) It is a divisible part of time.
B) It is a common boundary between past and future, like a point on a line.
C) It is an indivisible atom of time.
D) It is nonexistent like the past and future.

Key Takeaway
The now is a common boundary, not a part of time; this refutes the argument that time is nonexistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life between past and future, always in the now. But this lesson says the now is not a part of time—it's a boundary. If the now is only a fleeting moment, why do you cling to it as if it will last? How would you live differently if you truly believed every now is merely a vanishing point between what was and what will be?
?
The refutation argues that the past is not absolutely nonexistent; it exists in its own limit. Think of a sin you committed in the past. Do you treat it as absolutely gone, or do you acknowledge that it still exists in your record before Allah, awaiting either forgiveness or accountability? What action will you take today to address that past?
?
Imam Razi uses the analogy of a point on a line to explain the now. The point itself has no length, yet the line depends on it. In your prayer and devotion, do you treat each moment as a mere boundary that holds no weight, or as the very point through which your entire spiritual line—your journey to Allah—passes? Commit to making your next 'now' a conscious act of worship.

The Third Argument Against Time's Existence
Imam Razi presents and refutes the third proof that time would be necessary if existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against the existence of time and its refutation in Imam Razi's Muhassal.

Core Idea
Assuming time's non-existence after its existence already presupposes time, leading to a flawed argument.

Imam Razi presents a third proof that time cannot exist because it would lead to necessity, but then he shows the flaw in that reasoning. This passage is a classic example of logical argumentation in kalam, demonstrating both a proof and its critique. We will walk through the argument step by step, then see how the marginal note exposes its hidden assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice that the author first states the conditional and then explains why the assumption is impossible.
The argument is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The argument says: if time existed and could cease, then its non-existence would have a 'before' and 'after' that require time. This creates a contradiction.
3
Understand why the consequent is absurd
The argument concludes that time would have to be necessary (واجب الوجود), but the author immediately objects: time's parts are temporal and contingent, so the whole cannot be necessary.
This shows the first layer of critique.
4
Examine the marginal note's refutation
The marginal note (أقول) clarifies the fallacy: the error is in assuming time's non-existence after its existence, which already presupposes time. One can assume time's absolute non-existence without before/after, which is possible.
This is the deeper logical correction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : الزمان لو كان موجودا لسكان واجب الوجود لذاته وفساد التالى يدل على فساد المقدم ، وبيان الشرطية أنه او كان موجودا وفرضناه قابلا للعدم فليفرض أنه عدم فيكون عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا توجد مع القبل ، وهذه البعدية لا تتحقق إلا عند تحقق الزمان فإذا يلزم من فرض عدم الزمان وجوده وذلك محال. فإذا لمجرد فرض عدمه يستلزم المحال ، فإذا كان فرض عدمه محال فهو واجب لذاته .
وإنما قلنا أنه يستحيل أن يكون واجبا لذاته لان كل جزء منه حادث وممكن والمجموع ينقوم بالأجزاء والمنقوم بالممكن المحدث يستحيل أن يكون واجبا لذاته (۲)
أقول : فرض الزمان بعد و چوده يكون فرض عدمه مع وجوده ويلزم منه المحال لاشتماله على عدم الشيء ووجوده ، وفرض عدم الزمان وحده ممكن إذا لم يقترن ذلك العدم بقبل أو بعد ، وهذا الغلط نشأ من قياس الزمان على ما في الزمان ومن اقتران وجود الشيء بعدمه .
And the third [argument]: If time were existent, it would be necessary of existence in itself (واجب الوجود لذاته). The corruption of the consequent indicates the corruption of the antecedent. The explanation of the conditional: If it were existent and we assume it to be susceptible to non-existence, then let it be assumed that it becomes nonexistent. Its non-existence after its existence has an 'afterness' that does not exist with the 'before'. This 'afterness' is not realized except with the realization of time. Thus, from the assumption of the non-existence of time, its existence follows, and that is impossible. So merely the assumption of its non-existence entails an impossibility. Since the assumption of its non-existence is impossible, it is necessary of existence in itself. And we say that it is impossible for it to be necessary of existence in itself because every part of it is temporal (حادث) and contingent (ممكن), and the whole is constituted by its parts, and what is constituted by contingent, temporal things cannot be necessary of existence in itself. (2) I say: Assuming time's non-existence after its existence is assuming its non-existence with its existence, and that leads to impossibility because it involves both non-existence and existence of the same thing. But the assumption of time's non-existence alone is possible if that non-existence is not associated with a before or after. This error arose from analogizing time to things in time and from associating the existence of a thing with its non-existence.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the existence of time, followed by a refutation in the marginal note. The argument attempts to prove that if time existed, it would have to be necessary of existence (واجب الوجود), which is impossible because time consists of contingent, temporal parts. The argument uses a conditional: assume time exists and is possible to cease. If it ceases, its non-existence has a before and after relationship that itself requires time. Thus, the assumption of time's non-existence implies time exists—a contradiction. Therefore, time cannot be contingent; it would be necessary. But the author then points out that time cannot be necessary because its parts are all temporal and contingent, so the whole is contingent. The marginal note (أقول) clarifies the fallacy: the error arises from assuming time's non-existence after its existence (i.e., temporal succession), which already presupposes time. But one could assume time's absolute non-existence without reference to before/after, which is possible. Thus the argument is flawed.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
حادث
العدم
البعدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of time's non-existence (due to temporal succession) proves time necessary.
Correction: The impossibility only arises if we assume a temporal context for non-existence; time's absolute non-existence can be conceived without contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal note, what is the main fallacy in the third argument against time?
A) Time's parts are eternal, not contingent.
B) The argument assumes time's non-existence after its existence, which already requires time.
C) Time cannot be necessary because it is uncreated.
D) The argument confuses time with space.

Key Takeaway
The third argument against time fails because it illicitly imports temporal concepts into the assumption of time's non-existence, and time's composition from contingent parts shows it cannot be necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You habitually think of your own existence as having a 'before' and 'after'. But what if that very framework is an assumption you impose on reality? Would you be willing to let go of the security that comes from thinking of yourself as a continuous story, and instead see each moment as entirely contingent, without any necessary link to the next?
?
The passage says time cannot be necessary because its parts are temporal and contingent. You, too, are composed of moments — each one given and potentially taken away. Are you living as if you are necessary, building your life on the presumption of tomorrow? What would it mean to truly accept that you are contingent through and through, not just in theory but in your daily choices?
?
Imam Razi points out that the error is analogizing time to things in time. In your own spiritual life, are you guilty of measuring the eternal by the temporal — for example, thinking of God's attributes in terms of human limitations, or expecting your relationship with Him to follow a predictable timeline? What would change if you stopped forcing the infinite into your finite categories?

The Fourth Argument: Time Cannot Be a Measure of All Existence
Razi states the fourth argument: time's impossibility as a measure of all existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against the existence of time, which claims that time would have to measure both changing and permanent things, leading to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If time exists, it must measure both change and permanence — but no single measure can do both.

Imam Razi presents a powerful logical argument against the reality of time. He argues that time cannot exist because it would have to apply to both changing and permanent entities, which is impossible. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation.
Notice the parallel between motions and God's existence.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument starts with the premise that if time exists, it would be the measure of absolute existence.
3
Understand the contradiction
The argument concludes that time cannot apply to both changing and permanent, so it cannot exist.
This is a dilemma: time is either changing or permanent, both fail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : لو كان الزمان موجودا لكان مقدار المطلق الوجود، فإنا كما نعلم بالضرورة أن من الحركات ما كانت موجودة أمس أومنها ما يوجد غدا ، كذلك تعلم بالضرورة أن الله تعالى كان موجودا بالأمس ، وأنه موجود الآن وسيبقى موجودا غدا فإن جاز إنكار أحدهما جاز إنكار الآخر، لكن يستحيل أن يكون مقدارا لطلاق الموجود ، لأنه فى نفسه إن كان متجددا استحال انطباقه على الثابت ، وإن كان ثابتا استحال انطباقه على المتجدد (۳)
فإن قلت : نسبة التغير إلى المنغير هو الزمان ونسبته إلى الثابت هو الدهر ونسبة الثابت إلى الثابت هو السرمد.
أقول : لاشك فى أن وقوع الحركة مع الزمان ليس كوقوع الجسم القار الذات المستمر للوجود مع الزمان ، وليس كوقوع القار الذات الباقى مع القار الذات الباقى كالسماء مع الأرض ، وذلك للفرق معقول محصل سواء كان ذلك تهويلا أو غير تهويل، وليس معية المتغير والثابت مستحيلا ، فإنا نقول نوح عليه السلام عاش ألف سنة وانطبق مدة بقائه على ألف دورة من الشمس ، وإذا تقرر اختلاف المعاني فالمصطلحين أن يعبروا عن كل معنى بعبارة يرون أنها مناسبة لذلك المعنى، ولا يعنون بتحصيل هناك غير دلالة العبارات على المعانى .
ون
قلت : هذا التهويل خال عن التحصيل ، لأنى قد دلت على أن مفهوم كان ويكون ليس أمرا موجودا في الأعيان لكان إما أن يكون قار الذات فيلزم أن لا يوجد فى المتغيرات وإن كان متغيرا استحال وجوده في الثابت وهذا التقسيم لا يندفع بالعبارات (1)
أقول : لاشك فى أن وقوع الحركة مع الزمان ليس كوقوع الجسم القار الذات المستمر للوجود مع الزمان ، وليس كوقوع القار الذات الباقى مع القار الذات الباقى كالسماء مع الأرض ، وذلك للفرق معقول محصل سواء كان ذلك تهويلا أو غير تهويل، وليس معية المتغير والثابت مستحيلا ، فإنا نقول نوح عليه السلام عاش ألف سنة وانطبق مدة بقائه على ألف دورة من الشمس ، وإذا تقرر اختلاف المعاني فالمصطلحين أن يعبروا عن كل معنى بعبارة يرون أنها مناسبة لذلك المعنى، ولا يعنون بتحصيل هناك غير دلالة العبارات على المعانى .
And the fourth [argument]: If time were existent, it would be the measure of absolute existence. For just as we necessarily know that some motions existed yesterday and some will exist tomorrow, so too we necessarily know that God was existent yesterday, is existent now, and will remain existent tomorrow. If it is permissible to deny one, it is permissible to deny the other. But it is impossible for it to be the measure of absolute existence, because if it is in itself changing, it cannot apply to the permanent; and if it is permanent, it cannot apply to the changing. (3) If you say: The relation of change to the changing is time, its relation to the permanent is dahr (eternity), and the relation of the permanent to the permanent is sarmad (perpetuity). I say: There is no doubt that the occurrence of motion with time is not like the occurrence of a permanent, self-subsistent body with time, nor like the occurrence of a permanent, subsistent essence with another permanent like the sky with the earth. For the difference is rationally established, whether that is exaggeration or not. The coexistence of the changing and the permanent is not impossible. For we say: Noah (peace be upon him) lived a thousand years, and his duration of remaining corresponded to a thousand rotations of the sun. If the difference of meanings is established, then the terminologists should express each meaning with a term they see as suitable for that meaning, and they do not intend by that anything other than the indication of the expressions to the meanings. And I say: This grandiosity is devoid of substance, because I have shown that the concept of 'was' and 'will be' is not an existent thing in reals. If it were, it would either be permanent in essence, then it would not exist in changing things; or if it were changing, its existence in the permanent would be impossible. This division cannot be repelled by expressions. (1) I say: There is no doubt that the occurrence of motion with time is not like the occurrence of a permanent, self-subsistent body with time, nor like the occurrence of a permanent, subsistent essence with another permanent like the sky with the earth. For the difference is rationally established, whether that is exaggeration or not. The coexistence of the changing and the permanent is not impossible. For we say: Noah (peace be upon him) lived a thousand years, and his duration of remaining corresponded to a thousand rotations of the sun. If the difference of meanings is established, then the terminologists should express each meaning with a term they see as suitable for that meaning, and they do not intend by that anything other than the indication of the expressions to the meanings.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This lesson covers the fourth argument and subsequent objections and responses. The argument claims that if time exists, it would measure all existence, including both changing things (motions) and permanent things (God). But a single measure cannot apply to both changing and permanent. The objection ('فإن قلت') proposes distinguishing three terms: time for the changing, dahr for the permanent relative to change, and sarmad for permanent to permanent. The author responds (first 'أقول') that this is mere terminology; the logical division still stands. The opponent then retorts ('قلت') that the objection is empty. The second marginal note (again 'أقول') repeats the example of Noah, emphasizing that the coexistence of changing and permanent is not impossible. The passage stresses that the core issue is conceptual: time as a measure cannot simultaneously be applied to radically different kinds of existence.

Key Terms
مقدار المطلق الوجود
الثابت
المتغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think time is a real entity that can measure both change and permanence.
Correction: The argument shows that a single measure cannot apply to contradictory natures; time would have to be either changing or permanent, but not both.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the fourth argument?
A) Time is a measure of motion only.
B) If time exists, it must measure all existence, both changing and permanent.
C) Time is the duration of God's existence.
D) Time is the interval between events.

Key Takeaway
Time cannot exist because its nature cannot simultaneously accommodate both change and permanence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How much of your trust in God's control over your life is undermined by your reliance on time as a fixed framework for your plans? What would it mean for you to truly see time as unreal and Allah as the only constant?
?
Do you live as if time is a god that governs your successes and failures, or do you believe that Allah is independent of time and directly measures all existence? How would your daily anxieties change if you internalized this argument?
?
What would change in your daily decisions if you knew that time is not a real container but a mental construct, and that Allah's existence is unchanging? What step can you take today to live less enslaved to the clock and more anchored in the Eternal?
